This eminent Specialist, Fomider, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-

and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-

PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES,
lie has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may bo eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to -hit
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes, Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulnces, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back. Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,
weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking
cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,
wakefulness, palpitation of the heart, general
weakness, and other symptoms which lead to in-
sanity and death, should consult him, for ho has
mastered these difficulties. On receipt of 6d. in

plain wrapper, postage

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
'“Correspondence” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
“ The Bulletin" will return all unavailable MSS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings
sterling—which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our earners, with whom u-e do not wish
to interfere.

SATURDAY, JULY 18, 1891.
Wages.
The most generally accepted theory regarding
wages is that we are governed by the laws of sup-
ply and demand. This doctrine assumes that
there is a fixed proportion of the capital of every
country available for the payment of wages, and
if the supply of workers be decreased the share
which each individual will receive of this capital
will be greater, while on the contrary, if the
supply be augmented wages will fall. Thus, if the
wages fund,” as it is called, be £lO,OOO, and the
supply of workers 5000, each share of cash is £2; if
the number fall to 4000 the share of each is £2 10s.,
while if the workers are 7500, the share of each is
£1 6s. Bd. This is the theory, divested of its
verbiage, which is countenanced by Adam Smith,
Mill and the larger portion of their immediate
followers. In upholding it these writers main-
tain that the determination of the price of labour
differs in no respect from the determination of the
price of any other commodity, that all are alike
regulated by supply and demand, over the
laws of which the labourer has no control. No
one who has studied the question will gainsay the
truth of a portion, at least, of this- theory, vi?:.,
that wages, being the price of labour—that is to
say, the rates at which labour will exchange for
any other commodity—are therefore subject to the
same laws in regard to exchange as all cither com-
modities.
* * *
With all due respect to Mill and the others, to
say that prices are fixed by the laws of supply and
demand is palpably incorrect. If it were so, all
commodities, the supply of which exactly balances
the demand, must have an equal exchange
value; and any article for which there is
a greater demand than supply, must of neces-
sity be higher in price than another of which
the supply is in excess. We should accordingly
sometimes see pumukins selling at equal rates with
watches, and coal with silverware, that is if the
supply and demand were in each case equal. The
absurdity of the original theory has been suffi-
ciently obvious to induce the later economists of
this school to so, alter it that it now reads : “ The
ratio at which commodities will exchange is regu-
lated by the supply and demand at a price* and
competition determines what that price shall be.”
It would be beyond the scope of this article to
discuss the numerous contradictions which the
supply and demand theory involves; one or two
cases, however, may be given to illustrate our
meaning. Taking England, since it is thecountry
concerning which there is most information, it
may be broadly stated that there never was a time
within the last 400 years when the supply of
labour was not in excess of the demand; there-
fore, according to the Smith-Mill theory, wages
should have continuously tended to fall there,
whereas, the fact is, wages have constantly tended
upwards, in spite of the atrocious enactments by
which the English Parliament sought to repress
labour.
* # #
It is estimated by competent authorities that in
the palmiest days of American labour there are at
least five out of a hundred labourers who cannot
find work, while usually the proportion ranges
from seven and a-half to ten—and yet wages do
not fall. Some of our readers may remember the
days when the <ilite from Potts Point and Toorak
fought over the possession of the servant girls
imported by a complaisant country. There is no
gainsaying that the demand then exceeded the
supply, for 200 heated dowagers usually struggled
for 50 servants. We do not strive for arithmetical
accuracy, but it may be roughly deduced that in
such a case as the one just mentioned, where the
demand was four times the supply, wages should
have gone up four times. They did not, however;
they simply stood still.
« * *
The truth is, the law regulating prices is not
that of supply and demand. The ratiobetween the
amount of commodities and the demand, affects
their price, but does not determine it. What
does determine it is the cost of production. A
familiar example will best illustrate this. Suppose
all the butter consumed in the country is made
by five persons. A has inferior appliances and
can afford to sell at Is. per pound, B at lid., C at
10d., D at 9d., and E at Bd. So long as the
quantity produced is not in excess of the require-
ments, the price obtained will be Is., for as A’s
quantity is needed to complete the supply, and as
lie cannot sell below Is., what he must get for his
butter is what the others may and will get.
Should the production be increased beyond the
requirements of the community a portion must
remain unsold. This portion must necessarily be
A’s, since it is the dearest and the price will fall.
As A cannot continue making at a loss he will be
compelled to stop work, and B’s price of lid. will
regulate the market, but only so long as the
necessary supply is not exceeded by the production
of the makers; when it is exceeded prices will
again fall, and B will be driven out of competi-
tion, just as A was. This process will continue so
long as the production exceeds the supply, until
the lowest point (E’s price) is reached. Beyond
this the price cannot fall, for as'E cannot afford
to make butter at less than Bd., he will not do so,
at all events, for any long period. Should the
production by any chance exceed the demand
prices will, of course, instinctively fall below the
cost of production, but production will then cease,
and the normal cost of production will determine
the price of the commodity produced,
# # #
From this reasoning the law may be deduced
that the price of any commodity is relatively
determined by the cost of producing that portion
of the necessary supply which is produced under
the least advantage. That is while A’s produc-
tion was part of the necessary supply, A’s price
was the one which determined that of the whole
supply. This is the normal condition of things.
In ordinary practice, when B and the others find
that A cannot go below a certain price, and they
desire to increase their business by driving A out,
they fix the price at a point below his cost of pro-
duction, and if they are able to produce the requisite
quantity, this price, and not A’s, regulates the
market; but under no circumstances can any
maker continuously sell for less than the cost of
production, which cost is again fixed by the
labourer’s standard of living. Hence it is that
though a demand suddenly increased may enable
makers to obtain for a time a large advance on the
price at which they can afford to sell, a fall in the
demand will not bring prices below the level of
the cost of production.
« # *
We may, therefore, take it as a general law that
the cost of producing that part of the necessary
supply produced at the least advantage, deter-
mines the price of a commodity, and that prices
also tend downwards to the cost of the commodity
produced at the best advantage—that is, at the
cheapest rate. Labour is a commodity just the
same as any of the things which it helps to pro-
duce —it is, therefore, governed by the self-same
laws, so that the price of labour is determined by
the cost of producing that portion of the necessary
supply, which is produced at the least advantage.
What, then, is the labour utilised to the least
advantage? It is the labour of the worker who
has a family to support, and the labour which
costs least to utilise is that of the unmarried
worker.
# • *
No man with a family to support will consent
to work for less than will enable him to maintain
himself and family, according to the standard of
living to which they have been accustomed. It
is, therefore, evident that, so long as the labour
of men with families is part of the necessary
supply, so long will the cost of their support—
that is to say, the cost of producing their labour —
be the wages of their trades. Let it be imagined
that the workers are divided into two equal
bodies, married and single—the former are able
to support themselves and families on 40s. a week,
while the latter require but 255. Suppose, also,
there is work for the major part of these labourers,
then the wages will be 40s. for married and single
alike. Now, if the demand for labour falls so low
that there is employment for less than one-half,
the price of labour will tend downwards until it
reaches 255., because the labour of men who require
40s. to live forms no part of the necessary supply;
while below 255, wages cannot fall. Fortunately,
such a contingency is practically impossible, and
the standard of living, which the ordinary labourer
and his family enjoy, determines the wages that
he will receive.
# # #
Just here it may be well to meet a plausible
objection, which will be advanced against this
theory. It may be argued that it is the rate of
wages which determines the standard of living,
and not the reverse, as we have stated. A little
investigation will dispose of this objection.
Wages in America are uniformly high, so
high that the trades organisations have, with
the greatest difficulty, been able to maintain
them. The standard of comfort enjoyed by
the native workman is superior to that of any of
the European workers, consequently even the
best of these can improve their lot by emigrating
to American soil. As a result, the world has seen
an exodus of Irish, German, Italian, English,
and other European labourers to the new world.
Men who lived in Galway contentedly on “pota-
toes and point,” in Silesia on beer and black
bread, in Umbria or Calabria on oil, bread, or
macaroni, find themselves able, when translated
to Chicago or Charleston, to live in a style un-
thought of in their wildest dreams. The effect is
what might be readily anticipated. The ignorant
foreigner cannot at once adapt himself to the
standard of American comfort, except perhaps in
regard to food ; the wages he can earn are much
above his needs, and he either spends the surplus
in debauchery, or, working only for part of the
week, earns sufficient to keep him according to his
low standard of comfort. Thus the native Ameri-
can with difficulty maintains wages, at the point
which ho deems necessary for the proper support
of his family, confronted by toilers whose mode
of life is entirely different.
m m m
It is evident enough that the standard of living
fixes the wages, and not the wages the standard
of living. In this connection may be mentioned
the peculiar circumstance that the best workmen
of every country find difficulty in living in their
own country. Thus the best Americans or Aus-
tralians find their wages no more than they need
for their support, so, too, will the Englishman,
the German and the Italian when at home. It is
also worthy of remark that the Englishman whose
means of living are so narrow in his own land,
does well in America or Australia, the German
does well in England and saves, while the Italian
in Germany has also made a fortunate exchange
of fatherland.
* # #
It is an historical fact that when the free opera-
tion of labour has not been restrained by laws,
wages have advanced with civilisation. The laws
against labour in England were so severe during
three centuries, as almost to keep down the
natural tendency of the population to improve
their standard of comfort. With the French
revolution and the dawn of the factory system
came a new 'fcrder of things. Restrictions to
travelling were removed, social intercourse was
necessarily developed and the standard of living
rapidly rose, so that in a very short period wages
almost doubled. In Australia the same phenpiriena
are observable. In the good old days of beggary, a
day’s wage was about four shillings, men were bred
to believe that anything above a full convict
ration was a foretaste of paradise, were content
with a pittance, since it gave them all they needed.
With the roystering days of the gold- seekers came
men with other ideas. Their minds were not
bounded by the traditions of a convict regime.
Their money came easily and was spent freely,
and their legacy to Australia is a standard of
comfort higher than any to be found elsewhere.
# # *
The first duty of the labourer in this country, or
any other country, is not to save, as some of his
mentors would have him believe : it is to see that
the comfort of his family is not only maintained,
but increased. In so far as saving is an aid to
this, it is good, but when it tends to lower the
comfort of his class—that is, the standard of living
—it is a distinct evil. Men whose wages are
sufficient to enable them to indulge themselves
with a four-roomed house and to clothe their
families neatly and well, but who prefer living in
a two-roomed shanty where the common decencies
of life cannot possibly be preserved, and, for the
sake of a balance in the savings bank, permit their
children to go meanly clad, are not the self-deny-
ing heroes that some would pretend. They are
the_enemies of their children, of morality and of
their fellows, and are preparing for the time,
when what they now do voluntarily will have to
be done of necessity. Workmen of Australia,
your duty is to maintain high wages, rot that you
may save, but that you and your’s may live in
comfort.
* * #
A few words more and we have finished. It
may be argued, if wages are determined by the
standard of living, there is no limit to the wages
which labourers may demand —that if they choose
to live like lords they will be paid like lords. The
objection is apparently a strong one. A little ex-
planation,, however, and it vanishes. The price
of labour is governed by the cost of it to the pro-
ducer, this we have shown is the standard of
living which he enjoys. It is, however, governed
by something else—the produce of his labour. If,
then, after the cost of governing and theearn-
ings of capital be taken from the total produce,
there be sufficient when divided by the number of
labourers to enable each to live like lords, there is
nothing in the nature of things to prevent them
so living, for the capitalist is merely a social out-
growth that later generations may entirely dis-
pense with.
Another “National Party.”
When the Last Day arrives, and the heavens
and the earth fall together with a gruesome
smash, and the stars bolt in affright, and lost
comets dash round unexpected corners of the
firmament, and red-hot suns trample madly
over mislaid asteroids, and all is panic and dis-
organisation—then there will be a strange and
horrid voice heard among the ruins of the globe
voice of the last monopolist who is busy
founding an Association on top of the wreckage,
and passing resolutions to provide for the main-
tenance of Law and Order, and to promote the
influx of foreign capital, and the investment of
hard cash upon good shecurity. There is some-
thing sad and incurable about that monopolist,
and there is a hideous weariness about these
resolutions which he passes for the maintenance
of Law' and Order. He is so dull, so fat and
thick-headed, so hopelessly incapable of realising
that the whole universe wasn’t built for the sole
purpose of having capital invested in it, that it
would be utterly impossible even to blast a new
idea into his intelligence. When the outer
world asks for anything he looks at it with a
distracted and surprised air—the noise it
makes disturbs him in the process of investing
his capital. When the nation tries to do any-
thing or to reform anything he tells it to keep
quiet and let things slide, lest it should shake
up his capital, and he is filled with genuine
amazement because the rest of humanity doesn’t
take as much interest in his capital as he does
himself.
* # •
These reflections are aroused chiefly by the
conduct of an alleged “ National Association,”
which started business the other day in Mel-
bourne. Its avowed object is to be the germ of
a great “National Party,” and the first begin-
ning of a vast upheaval which will pulverise
the rising Labour-party, and its stock-in-trade
consists of one or tw r o resolutions, about forty
overgrown stomachs, and one cablegram. For
the Association, at the close of its first meeting,
cabled itself to England as being the great
unanimous outburst of the Australian" people,
and Baron Reuter humped this baseless asser-
tion along at the customary rates. There are
seven purposes in the Association’s programme,
and all of them are as vague and undefinable as
an unsupported statement up a tree, but they
have all a high-class and gentlemanly tendency,
and though they are somewhat thin and sloppy,
none of them seem likely to bring a blush to
the most modest cheek. They run as follow's—
or, rather, being too feeble to run, they sit
down-and flop aimlessly
1. To preserve the national character of the Legislature,
the association affirming that representatives should not
be the delegates of any section of society.
2. To oppose all unconstitutional political procedure,
and thereby assist in maintaining law and order, and
particularly to. secure the effectual protection of all
persons engaged in honest industry.
3. To assert and defend the right of every person in the
community to dispose of his labour and capital with
perfect freedom within the law.
4.. To prevent undue interference with the great pro-
ducing interests of the colony, to encourage co-operation
and good feeling amongst all classes, to further to the
utmost the development of the agricultural, pastoral,
mining, and manufacturing interests, and to vigorously
oppose all class legislation injurious thereto.
0. To promote in all ways peace, security and general
confidence, in order that the public credit may be sus-
tained and the investment of capital encouraged, that a
maximum of employment may he provided, and that all
interests may share in a common prosperity.
6. To uphold a just balance of electoral representation,
giving proper weight to the claims of country districts.
upon the progress of which national life greatly depends.
7. To secure the due registration of Parliamentary
electors and the regular and systematic revisian of the
electoral-rolls.
* * #
The first thing that strikes the eye in this
programme is the desire of the Association “to
preserve the national character of the Legisla-
ture.” What the “national character” may
be is not stated, and where the object may be
lying round isn’t mentioned. The character of
the Victorian Legislature for seven years nast
has consisted principally of the fact that
Gillies’ cash wouldn’t square, and that Bryan
O’Loghlen has been Opposed to everything, and
the other characteristics have been aimless
whoopings about “ loyalty,” and a desire on the
part of everybody to get a railway constructed
with loan-money. The character of the people,
on the other hand, has consisted principally of
football, and, as a. natural consequence, there is
a discrepancy somewhere ; but it doesn’t matter
much. Anyhow, the Association proposes to
preserve the thing, whatever it may be ; and to
suppress “ class representation.” As everybody
must belong to some class, and as every class
has always tried to .secure all the representa-
tion it could get, and has wanted more after it
got it, this is a somewhat large order-; and
when it is promulgated by a class of forty or
fifty large-stomached men whose object is to
grab all the representation that is going, it
becomes still larger.' But that doesn’t matter
much either. Society, in a general way, is
divided into two sections, the first comprising
one per cent, of the population, and the other
ninety-nine ; and “class representation ” is the
state of things which prevails when the ninety-
nine per cent, is running the machine of State
and the one per cent, is cursing because it isn’t
running it instead.
* # *
The Association is also going to show a solid
front against all “ unconstitutional procedure,”
and as no one knows, or ever has quite known,
what the constitution—which is unwritten—is,
this loose statement may pass without much
attention. In a general sense, constitutional
procedure is the procedure of the man who is
speaking, and unconstitutional procedure is the
procedure of the other man. It is an open
question whether anybody ever was constitu-
tional since the world started, or whether any-
body ever was unconstitutional either, but that
is a matter of merely passing importance. The
problem might be settled some day if anybody
could find the constitution, but nobody ever
will find it, for there isn’t any such thing in
existence. Samples of it have been dug out
occasionally in the ‘Supreme Court and the
Privy Council, but the whole, original article
can’t be dropped upon anywhere. Further,
according to clause 3, the forty fat men intend
to assert the right of every man “ to dispose of
his labour and capital with perfect freedom
within the law.” This means, we presume, that
the one side can strike if it wants to, and that
the other side can see it stew in its own gravy, if
it chances to feel that way inclined, and they are
both doing it without any assistance from the
Association. Also, they have have both been
doing it more or less for several thousand years,
and will probably do it for several thousand
more, and the Association can’t make them do
it or make them stop doing it any more than
it can regulate the price of birds-nest soup in
Mongolia. And, anyhow, it is an asinine pro-
cedure for any Association to form itself for the
purpose of letting people do what they have
done all along; and that body of capitalists
would have acted a good deal more wisely if it
had organised itself into a powerful society,
with a strong reserve fund, for the purpose of
letting it rain in wet weather and for allowing
the thunder to roll when it wanted rolling.
* # «
Still, however, The Bulletin has no desire
to discourage any “National” organisation,
and the statement in clause 4, that the new
society will do its best to “ further the agri-
cultural, pastoral, mining, and manufacturing
interests,” is in every way commendable. It is
not to be supposed, of course, that the Associa-
tion will put up a cent to assist any of these
interests, except so far as it makes money out
of them, but it will lend them its countenance
and patronage—it will give three cheers when
it sees anybody digging potatoes, and will
afford its moral support to the individual whose
back is nearly broken through hauling up a
bucket of wash-dirt out of a shaft. Probably
enough the agricultural and mining interests
will resent this encouragement, and will rush
after the Association with a board, in which
case that body will doubtless bolt for' the
nearest place of safety, and there draw up a
report and a vote of thanks. It is also intended
to “ encourage good feeling among all classes,”
which may be defined as an intention to
encourage all classes to feel the way the
Association feels, and to discourage any
class which feels the way the Association
doesn’t feel. The Association’s own feeling is a
feeling .of stuffed sensation under the waistcoat,
arising from too many dinners, and it will in-
cite all other people to have the sajne symptoms
at their own expense, and will, discountenance
anybody who experiences a < hollowness in the
region of the stomach. And,’above all things, it
intends to promote “peace, security, and
general confidence,’ and “the investment of
capital.” It wants peace because it is un-
pleasant to be hit in the wind just after seven
courses ; it wants security because it is too fat
to run; and it-pines after general confidence
because confidence is a blessed thing at all
times, and leads the poor to put their savingtfin
the busted building society and the bottomless
mining swindle, and to buy up allotments at
boom prices, and to believe in time-payment
furniture, and to get left in all manner of com-
mercial frauds out of which the National Asso-
ciationists get considerable pickings. Also, it
supports the investment of capital for reasons
that are too obvious to be worth mentioning.
* * *
Taking it all round, the new institution
doesn’t Took like a matter that will do much
damage to the rising cause of Labour. Even
its resolution to secure “a just balance of elec-
toral representation ” doesn’t seem to amount
to much, for the man who gets most votes will
still get in after the same mean, underhand
fashion as of old ; and the desire to give i \


general support to “ the national life ” is some-
what indefinite. The national life will probably
crowd through without the help of the Associa-
tion, and if it can’t, it is too impalpable a thing
to allow of that body shoving it from behind—
it would be about as easy to shove a disease, or
to crowd a popular impression through a key-
hole, or to run down the Voice of the Past and
grasp it by the tail. The new organisation,
considered in all its bearings, seems to be a
good deal of a mule. Its stock-in-trade consists
of sundry fat men, seven platitudes, and
a cablegram. Its objects, as defined in the
advertisement, are “to maintain law, order,
and good Government,” but the country hasn’t
much good Government to maintain, and order
is all right and doesn’t need maintenance, and
the Labour-party is also bent on maintaining
} aw __tliat is, it means to get some new laws,
and to maintain them after it has got them.
The only asset worth mentioning which the
hew gang possesses is thetrenlendous gall which
has led it to christen itself the “ Victorian
National Party.” If unmitigated cheek can
drag ah Association through this is obviously
the sort of Association that will get through
every time.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Shearers’ Republic.
The Brisbane Courier is dishing up for the
hundred and fiftieth time the gruesome narra-
tive of that Shearers’ Republic which was just
going to happen, but didn’t take place, in
Queensland, owing to the fact that it failed to
come off on account Of circumstances which have
been frequently explained, and which have
grown more and more murky with each explana-
tion; The Courier’s story (supplied by amilitary-
officer who was formerly a newspaper-man) of the
Reign of Terror that fell through is muddled and
incoherent, but by piecing together the ruins and
fragments of its statements the matter stands
about thus:—The plan was con-
structed in a damp cavern at midnight. There
was no moon, and the wind howled dismally on
the mountains, and the air was as dark as a
hypocrite’s Sunday tail-coat at 12 p.m., and the
rain sloshed down with a dreary slosh upon the
dead leaves underfoot. After awhile the boss
of the Shearers’ Union could be heard approach-
ing through the trees, and blaspheming horribly
as he came ; also he was gnashing his teeth and
foaming at the mouth. The remains of a dead
capitalist were sticking out of his pocket, and
he dragged a murdered and partially-eaten
banker behind him. He had eight limbs alto-,
gether, and travelled like an immense spider*
and he had one bloodshot eye, about the size of
a cocoa-nut, in the middle of his forehead J
in addition to which he had a scalded
look about him, and was drunk and caked
all over with mud. The other party to
the transaction was waiting for him in the cave.
He was shaped like an octopus, and smelt hor-
ribly of sulphur, and he was covered with a
shiny crust that looked like a mixture of
squashed black beetles, old fish, and glue. Be-
sides these physical advantages, he had a tail
which was evidently hot, for it emitted a fizzing
sound when he dropped it into a pool of water,
and once or twice he put it out in the rain to
cool. A phosphorescent light shone all over
him, and when he sneezed he burnt a hole in his
pocket-handkerchief. These two parties ar-
ranged between them for the establishment of
the Republic, and it was resolved that Moreton
Bay was to be stood up on end to run the water
off and thus destroy all commerce, and that
every person who owned any property was to
be split open, and the germs of industry picked
out of him and destroyed. All individuals of
loyal tendencies were to be disembowelled, and
any man who admired Queen Victoria was to
be put in a bag along with eight cats, some
snakes, and a grindstone, and dropped into the
sea amid the cheers of the Shearers Union and
wild applause from the Trades and Labour
Council. The two contracting parties then
jumped for a long time on the remains of the
dead capitalist, and —ent away using bad lan-
guage. This is Hi.; i crier’s story, put into
as coherent langu id ■ as tlie natural incoherence
of that journal wiU allow. It is always a plea-
sure to help the Courier out of a difficulty 4 it
is printed on such thick paper that everybody
likes it.
“A Jerry-Built Constitution.”
That old, useless, fat man, the “ representative
Australian,” has broken out afresh in England
over the approaching —or the said-to-be ap-
proaching —Commonwealth. He has broken
out several times already over the same scheme,
sometimes because the draft Constitution wasn’t
loyal enough, sometimes because it wasn’t suffi-
ciently British in its appearance, sometimes
because it didn’t follow on the lines of that old
constitutional groveller who built up the Cana-
dian Dominion, sometimes because the project
was too long, and at other times because it was
too thin, this time, however, the “represen-
tative kawlinist” writes in the St. James’
Gazette, under the heading: “ A Jerry-built
Constitution,” to complain about yet another
trouble; —
Confederations, Bunds, Leagues, and Covenants, of
winch the world has heard much, have been conceived
and horn in travail and suffering ; sanctified by shed
blood, by resolution, fortitude, and self-sacrifice. Mighty
and famous governmental fabrics, after all, are horn, not
made. No tragic or troublesome elements enter into the
mild caprice for Colonial Federation in the South Seas.
This is by no means a new objection—in fact, it
is getting to be a very old one. A good many
“representative Australians” have lodged a
complaint that there were no “ tragic elements”
on the stage when the Federation scheme was
initiated —in other words, that nobody was.
killed, and no city was set on lire, and that,
there was no horrible invasion with famine and
pestilence behind it to boom the Federal idea
along. And the “representative” mud-head
seems to make no allowance for the fact that
Australia had no “ tragic elements ” on hand at
the moment, barring the torpedo-accident in
Sydney Harbour and the bursting of the big
gun at Melbourne ; and it couldn’t get any.
Apparently the theory is that because most of
the great States of the past started with murder
and fire, therefore it is impossible to build a
new State at any time in the future, unless
murder and the other things are thrown in
according to custom—in other words, the Com-
monwealth won’t hold together unless shells are
bursting in George-street, Sydney, and some
army is getting licked on the Darling Downs.
It was of materials like these that “Confedera-
tions, Bunds, Leagues, and Covenants ” were
constructed, and a horribly bad lot they were.
At least the principal Confederation that we
remember just now lasted through four years
of slaughter, and then died miserably ; and the
Bund that left the biggest mark on history was
a loose gang of several hundred crowned idiots,
and was a hideous failure from beginning to
end ; and the League and Covenant that occurs
to us at the present moment didn’t lead to
union, but disunion, and after 40 years of howl-
ing bigotry in the pulpit and hopeless collapse
in the battlefield, it perished of premature old
age and a rush of misapplied Scripture to the
head. The world certainly “heard much” of
all of them, and it doesn’t want to hear any-
thing like it again. Moreover, the people of
Australia don’t care one rap whether the
world “hears much” about their Corhmon-
wealth or not, for the only things that are much
heard of in history are kings, wars, and sieges;
battles, massacres; wreckage; broken treaties;
and sudden death; and Australia must either
lay in these things or be comparatively unheal’d
of—consequently it doesh’t want to be “ heard.”
The representative Australian has got his idea
upside-down. The “ Confederations; Bunds,”
«&c., of the past were “ born in travail and suf-
fering,” and thfey were such a horribly mis-
shapen lot when they grew up that the
Australian Republic which is coming all
the time, day and night; means to try
the experiment of being born quietly, ill the
hope that it may lead to better results. And
the Leagues ‘ ‘ of which the world has heard
much ” have had just one thing the matter with
them —the world seldom heard any good of any
of them ; and therefore the Australian League
would rather stand out from the gang and
travel on a basis of its own.
♦
The “ People’s Party ” in the United
States.
This is the day of Third Parties in politics; and
still another one has just been born. The
people of the United States have grown tired
at last of the old, useless, purposeless scramble
between Democrats and Republicans —these two
dreary, monotonous factions whose insane
clawings after office never do good to anybody
except the office-holders, and whose policies are
as undefinable as ghosts scuffling in a fog ; and
at a great conference held the other day at
Cincinnati the foundations were laid of a new
organisation, to be called the Party of the
People-. This third section is already so strong
that it seems likely to command eight States at
the next election, and it is gaining new
accessions daily. Its programme is that it cares
nothing at all for Republicans or Democrats,
and would gladly see them all sunk in the
deepest part of the Atlantic—that it won’t
whoop for Harrison or Cleveland, because
the country would be better without either of
them—but that it means to burst up big estates
and wipe out the whole race of millionaires by
means of a graduated income-tax and ponderous
succession duties—that it declares war against
the huge railway-monopolies and demands that
they should be replaced by Government pro-
prietorship—and that it wants a system of
State-banks, shorter hours of labour, and no
more Government by Capital. In short, it Is
the nearest approach to a Labour-party that
has yet appeared in America, and the old Re-
publicans and Democrats are filled with indigna-
tion. They have scrambled for office so long
and so furiously that it has become a recognised
principle that the jobs and the pickings must
belong to one of them, and the idea that the
country may one day do without both of them
is a fact that is too large for their compre-
hension. Each of them has laid down the
principle that when the other gang of brigands
is in the nation is lost, and when its own gang
of brigands is in the nation is saved, and the
mass of the nation is not one cent, better oft
when it is saved than when it is lost. Apart
from the billets and the pickings, and the horrible
language which they throw at each other, there is
no visible difference between a Democrat and a
Republican nowadays. The two parties divided
principally on a question that was settled
twenty-five years ago, but though they both
accepted the settlement, and thus left them-
selves without a single decent point of distinc-
tion to travel on, they have somehow induced
the country to spend all its efforts for many
years in shoving them into office turn-about.
The country can’t tell them apart so far as
concerns any good it ever gets out of them;
and if the “People’s Party grows strong it is
more than probable that they will suddenly
realise that their enmity was a mistake all
along, and that they are both really the same
party and always have been—the Party of
Capital as distinguished from that of Labour
and Humanity. When the People begin to
legislate for themselves there is always a
sudden and unexpected unanimity among their
enemies. The Liberals and Conservatives of
Victoria are so absolutely alike that it is almost
necessary to put a dab of paint on one lot for
the sake of distinction:; the “Great Liberal
Party ” and its opponents in Queensland can
only be distinguished by their clothes:; the
British Tory and the British Liberal are just
as far apart as Smyth is from Smith and no
further; and, until the Democrat and the
Republican start to curse each other, no one
can tell which is the one and which is the
other.
Beaney’s Last Will and Advertisement.
Dr. Beaney’s will is a touching record of that
posthumous charity which vaunted not itself,
and is not puffed up. The four £IOOO bequests'
to Melbourne University (2), Guy’s and St.
Thomas’ Hospitals, go towards perpetuating the
glorious name of Beaney in four scholarships,
£IO,OOO issetapart for the erection of a “Beaney
Institute for the education of Working Men,”
in the hero’s native city of Canterbury, and
sundry personal relics, including two volumes
of “ Men of the Time ” (ye gods !), and a photo,
of himself in fancy-dress (again, ye gods .) are
bequeathed to the said Institute for future
generations to gloat over. Beaney’s Melbourne
tombstone is to swallow up a modest £I2OO of
the estate for certain, but the question of a
further £I2OO tablet in Canterbury Cathedral
has yet to be settled. It is like tins:
Beaney’s will undertakes to contribute £IOOO
to the repairing of that venerable pile on
condition that the £I2OO tablet is per-
mitted to be fixed up to Beaney’s memory m
the Cathedral aisle. The main object of this
munificence is to mix up Beaney with Thomas
a’Beoket and the Black Prince in the minds of
visitors, and it strikes one as odd that the
doctor didn’t devote a large sunt of money
to subsidise an ailnual pilgrimage to
his blessed tablet. Even Beaney, however
entertained doubts as to whether he could
purchase immortality in the oldest of
English churches, for he specially men-
tioned that, if the Dean and Chapter refuse
to let Beaney mend the roof on his own terms,
the £IOOO for repairs and £I2OO for tablet are
to lapse into his residuary estate. The Bulle-
tin fears that they will lapse. Less worthy indi-
viduals thaii Beaney have been favoured with
memorials in Canterbury Cathedral, ho doubt.
But, on the other hand, Great Britain and her
kawlinies are full, of Vainglorious snobs who
would pay tefa times BeanEy’s price for the
little privilege he hankered after. Poor old
champagne Doctor! His former toadies and
flatterers are jeerihg at his “ charity since
they discovered that he has left them out in the
cold. They stand at street corners and curse
his miserable conceit, forgetting that he would
willingly have divided his too-easily-earned
money amongst them colild they have entered
into a solemn undertaking to bawl his name
from the housetops all day long until the Crack
of Doom and present him with an illuminated
address upon the Throne of Grace.

“ Re a Gentleman One,” as usUal. The other
day the New South Wales Pull Court considered
the case of a solicitor who had been entrusted
with £934 11s. by Cornelius Duffy, and had
stuck to the amount. And after duly thinking
over the situation the Court arose like a giant
refreshed, and the following words issued from
its head >
The Cjurt considered that it would be Very hard if
Duffy were compelled to pay this money over again
owing to the negligence and misconduct of his agent, the
respondent, who had retained the money and evidently
applied it to his own purposes. If the applicant had to
pay this money the second time, it only showed how
essential it was that solicitors of the Court should he men
of the utmost probity and possessing the highest sense of
honour.
The amount of light which a Full Court can
shed on an otherwise dull and commonplace
occurrence, when once it considers the matter
in dead earnest, is really surprising. Probably
no one would have thought of the above facts if
the Court hadn’t mentioned them. But then
suppose, for example, that the attorney, instead
of being an individual who was paid by Duffy
to look after his interests, and on whom Duffy
had a natural claim in consequence, had been a
mere outside practitioner who was under no
obligation to Duffy at all ; and suppose he had
got in at Duffy’s window in the centre of the
night, and fled with £934 11s., and had after-
wards been captured by Officer X. outside ;
and then supposing the Court, instead of rending
the plunder from the Attorney, and sending
him to gaol for five years, had solemnly remarked
that it was very rough on Duffy, and that
the event only showed that people in general
ought to have ’ a high sense of honour, so as to
prevent them burgling Duffy’s premises in the
night; supposing all these things now .

The Advantages of the Gallows.
One day the other week a member of the Victorian
Assembly called upon Chief-Secretary Shiels to
state whether he would introduce a Bill for
enabling condemned prisoners to choose their
own style of execution, just as they are, under
existing arrangements, permitted to put a name
to whatever they fancy for breakfast on the
eventful morning. Shiels threw cold water on
the idea, and pointed opt several objections to
any modification of the one-man-cme-rope
principle which is such an honoured bulwark of
our Liberties. In the first place, explained
Shiels, the death-penalty would be robbed of
much of its ignominy if the prisoners at the
bar were allowed a voice in the matter,
and ignominy is “ an essential element ”
of the wiping-out system, the one great aim of
the law being to make the victim feel as much
sense of moral degradation as possible wflen the
clock strikes ten. Then again, simpered the
waggish Chief-Secretary, whose obvious popu-
larity with the Collius-street girls we used to
envy in times that are no more, “they might
probably find not two human beings would
choose the same mode of execution, and ,tl)e
result would be , that .they would haye to
employ an executioner of varied attain-
ments (a Jack Ketch of all trades, so to
speak) or if they could not get such a personage
they would have to employ a large staff of exe-
cutioners in different styles. Whereupon the
House laughed immoderately at the notioa of,
say, a gross of Nosey Bobs, with power to add
to their,number, and the question dropped with
a dull, sickening thud. Now, although The
Bulletin vigorously objects to capital punish-
ment of any kind, and despises hanging for its
own clumsy sake, no proposal to introduce
variety into the horrors of judicial slaughter
can ever hope to enjoy the consistent support of
.this journal until it publishes “speshul heditions”
at a penny. In that case, The Bulletin might
be glad to compete with other evening papers
in picturesque descriptions of how the shudder-
ing culprit refused to drink the cup of cold
prussic acid he had selected to expiate his
abominable crime, and how his mouth had to be
held open by two sturdy warders whilst the
sheriff administered the fatal draught. It would
also givens great satisfaction to be first in the field
with a gruesome word-picture of the prisoner’s
anguish whilst being burnt alive according to
contract, or of the stolid indifference with which
he met his self-chosen doom of a gash across
the jugular artery. But, in the meantime, we
have no sympathy with any scheme for increas-
ing public interest in executions, or inciting
semi-lunatics to commit murders for the mere
pleasure of giving the world another novelty in
the “ turning-off” way. Shiels didn’t touch
upon the really convincing reasons why the
death-penalty cannot be left open td the discre-
tion of those chiefly interested in it; For
instance, he forgot td remind his honourable but
addle-pated friend that a Judge, after putting
on the black-cap, would have to pause
when he arrived at “ the sentence of
this Court is that you be;” and ask, in h
feeble; apologetic tone of voice — !< What?” Nine
prisoners out of ten would be too much upset
to mention straight away what kind df death
they would prefer; and the relatives and friends
present would start whispering suggestions of a
practical, but painful nature. The tenth
criminal would probably tell the learned judge
to “find out,” or say that he Wanted to be
chucked off the Eiffel To wet With all the
gentlemen df the jury tied round his neck, and
a scandalous scene in Court would naturally
ettsue. Moreover, even after a condemned
prisoner had selected his fate, he would be
continually regretting that he hadn’t gone ill
for something different, and the deputations to
the Governor would become most monstrous;
The present System relieves a condemned
prisoner’s mind of all worry and anxiety. The
Law says he will be hanged, in plain straight-
forward language, and whenpie is hanged it’s
just what he expected all along.
The Oath.
The other day, at Deniliquin (N.S.W.), the
customary Mongolian testifier was in the box
swearing to tell the truth, thfe whole truth, and
nothing but the truth, after the wonted fashion
of an Australian court of justice. Theh thfe
constable; or court-orderly, or some other
official, towed in a rooster to lend weight to the
evidence, and thfe Chinaman proceeded to hack
the head from the unhappy bird in order to
finish the oath and make it binding on his com
science. Next, the partially-murdered fowl
escaped and gambolled madly through the
premises scattering gore and feathers in all
directions, while the heathen witness went
wildly in pursuit in order to finish his
oath and put his evidence on a solid and credible
basis. For, having started to swear on the
mangled bird, it was necessary to finish the oath
before the witness’s testimony could be lawfully
received, and he couldn’t finish the oath unless
he overtook the escaped rooster —consequently,
the course of justice really depended on whether
a Chinaman or a distracted bird could run the
faster. This sort of thing is getting justice
down pretty low, and, in order to make the Law
look a trifle less of a fool than it usually does,
it might be almost worth while to abolish the
judicial oath entirely, whether it is sworn on a
Bible or a fowl, or any other object or substance
whatsoever. Why this old superstition was
ever started it is difficult to guess. It was sup-
posed at one time that a witness who had kissed
an old infected Scripture would be scared there-
after lest he should go to Sheol if he failed to
tell the truth, but the result shows that he
doesn’t care for the alleged danger to any extent
worth mentioning. In every case, large and
small, where evidence is called, the witnesses
contradict each other with a frankness and
vigour that clearly proves one side is lying, and
which does not prove that the other side isn't
lying also. And as, in many cases, the witnesses
are swearing out of pure friendship, and have
not the slightest financial interest in the matter,
it is evident that the world is full of
people who will fake their chance of
,Sheol to oblige a mere passing acquaintance,,
and the man who takes an oath is no more
reliable in consequence of that proceeding than
he would be if he had only taken a drink or an
onion. Moreover, the old formula is mischievous
as well as useless. When a child-witness is put
in the box the Court straightway tests fts
credibility by enquiring -if it knows where it
will go if it violates the oath ; and if the answer
comes in the one word “ Hell,” the evidence is
acceptable—not otherwise. In other words,
the infant that knows about Hell can swear
away the life or liberty of a human being, and
if another infant that doesn’t know about that
same institution is at hand ,to swear in his
defence, it will be rejected, pud the prisoner
might hang as a consequence. Yet horn mop-
sense and experience have long sipeje demon-
strated that the piore an infant Knows about
perdition the less likely is it to Vie reliable, for
instruction on that subject is to de-
moralise any infant; and, inoreover, as neither
the Law nor the Church nor all the colleges have
ever yet proved that there is any perdition at
all, the'legal test of credibility is that the
infant-witness must know a .thing which pro
; bably doesn’t exist at all—iii otljer words, it
ipust lie as a proof that it tells the truth. The
dismal old process is good for three,things only
—it disseminates germs by means of' ancient
Bibles and thus helps the struggling medical
professipp—it furnishes sundry half-crowns for
commissioners of affidavits—and in places where
,Chinese witnesses are plentiful it booms the
chicken industry considerably. But no adult
witness that wanted to lie ever lied any the less
because of his oath ; and as far as concerns the
infant or child-testifier, this crippled supersti-
tion shuts out the only kind of child that ever
does tell the truth—the child who hasn’t heard
about Hell and been demoralised in the hearing.

“ Agitators.”
Now that some thirty or forty vigorous
“ agitators ” have invaded the New South
Wales Legislature, the monopolist press has
toned down a good deal in its language, and
the epithet at the he:td of this item isn’t heaved
with anything like its old regularity. This
sort of thing makes The Bulletin feel sorry.
As far back as the oldest inhabitant can
remember, and away further back still, into the
buried ages of that oldest inhabitant's remote
ancestors, every individual who has’attempted
to stir the Dead Sea of humanity by arousing
new thoughts and aspirations, has been de-
nounced as an “agitator.” The term has
( always been regarded as the last possible hunk

W, J. DOUGLAS, Manager,
of infamy that could be heaped on any human
being ; when he didn’t mind being called a
cannibal, a Hottentot, or a parricide, this final
insult was fired at his head, and if he proved
to be proof against that, then language was
exhausted and the dictionary was used up.
The old,; tired expression has been thrown at
Jesus Christ, Paul, Peter, Barnabas,
Luther, Mahomet, Savonarola, Rienzi,
Hancock,- Wilberforce, John Huss, Tren-
With,Gladstone, the Four Evangelists, the
Shearers-’ Union, the prophet Jeremiah,
Knox ; Fitzobert, the “ King of the Poor ”;
Kossuth,- Jack Cade, Wat Tyler, Parnell,
Garibaldi, and everybody else who has been of
any use to mankind ; and the Labour-party has
grown so used to it that they will feel the want
of the old curse when it falls into oblivion. For
it is no small honour to Fitzgerald and Han-
cock and their colleagues, that they should be
cursed by the modern capitalist with exactly
the same anathema that the ancient capitalist
discharged at the great Redemeer in Judaea ;
and, surprising as it may seem, they rather
like it. Nobody ever did any great good to
this world without being an agitator of some
description. Nobody ever did a great action
without causing agitation among that class
which hates greatness, and detests action of
any kind, and whose only ambition is to be an
imported peer, and to stagnate in the same old
swamp in which its ancestors stagnated in the
days of primeval ignorance. Nobody ever
started a new idea without agitating somebody.
No one ever abolished an old abuse except by
being an agitator. The extinction of Pagan-
ism iu Europe, the abolition of slavery,
the destruction of the Inquisition all
were carried out by agitators. It was
a race of objectionable agitators who wiped
out depotism in England by means of
two. revolutions—who overturned the Bourbons
in France, and the throne of Bomba in Naples,
and who led to the disuse of the rack and the
thumbscrew and the wheel and similar things
that would have existed yet but for persistent
agitation. And the agitators who did these
things looked just as small and as commonplace
before they succeeded as Hancock or Fitzgerald
looks to the S.M. Herald to-day. Time and
distance make all the difference. Even the
Herald speaks respectfully of Kossuth, and
yet he was only a more violent kind of Han-
cock a long way off. The bloated Argus des-
cribes Grey as a “ venerable mischief-maker ”
and a “demagogue,” yet Grey is only a new
edition of Rienzi or Garibaldi under altered
Circumstances, and because these two great
Italians are both dead, and were a great dis-
tance away when they were alive, the Argus
looks on them as two respectable patriots. An
“ agitator,” in short, as the term is used by the
capitalist press, is a live patriot, and a patriot
is a dead “agitator,” and a hero is an “agita-
tor ” who is deader than a patriot. And,
therefore, the Labour-party does not feel
obliged to the organs of wealth because they
use their old curse a little less frequently since
the New South Wales election came off. The
Labour-party rather liked the curse than other-
wise. It wants to see the aged anathema going
ahead again in all its pristine vigour, and if
it doesn’t go ahead there will be trouble. This
is not meant as a threat, but as a friendly warn-
ing. Unless the Herald denounces the Labour
members as agitators and demagogues sometime
within the next week, something will happen to
that paper that will make it feel sorry. And
if the Herald or the Courier or the Argus is
found using terms of commendation about these
same Labour members either now or hereafter,
it will have cause to wish it had never been
born. The Labour-party doesn’t want to be
commended by any of them.

Dear Bulletin, — While cheerfully recog-
nising the fact that The Bulletin’s chief mission
on this sublunary sphere is to heave angular frag-
ments of “Plain English” at the heads of all
erring wanderers from the straight and narrow
path of democratic rectitude, I hardly see why
you should waste any ammunition of that sorb
upon your friends. That little yarn about my
having dined with Premier Parkes is one of the
many°fairy tales so industriously circulated of late
by interested parties, who handle “ Truth ” with
the most culpable negligence—not to put too fine
a point upon it. But it is really rather rough to
see The Bulletin following suit. I have never
received, much less accepted, any invitation to
partake of the hospitalities of Hampton Cottage
(or Villa—which is it?), and the alleged “little
civilities ” to which you refer are of an equally
imaginary character. If the par. had appeared in
any averqgo relia(r)ble journal, I should have let
it Jk. j.iov but as our crowd have a way of believing
anything that comes from The Bulletin office, I
must ask you to kindly “back down.”—Yours, in
the Cause, Frank Cotton.

My Dear Mooracinua
With an approving smile, Yours Ever rises to compli-
ment Sydney society on the twopence-coloured appear-
ance it presented at the Sara B. pri’aniCre at .Her
Majesty’s on Bth inst. Of course, there were one or two
“ little rifts”—one woman in red velvet and pink silk and
tawdry gilt lace looked Evangeline-y, but, on the whole,
the crowded d.c. and reserved stalls were up to date sar-
torially. The“'Etham” theatre party in the V.R. box
included Lady Charles Scott—in a black gown draped
with pink chiffon,—and Lord “ Precedent” Scott, one of
the ladies in the party wearing the best-looking diamonds
in the house. The handsomest dress in the circle (worn
by the worst-painted woman) was in salmon-pink silk
beautifully smocked and shirred and gauged on the
bodice, which had long Mandarin sleeves of pale-blue and
pink brocade interwoven with silver tinsel. Myra Kemble
(who doesn’t aid her resemblance to Sara by sliding eye or
copied coiffure) wore black with cluster of pink and cream
roses; Carrie Swain, pale-blue satin and diamonds. A
good many tinsel brocades were sported, and when the
light w T as turned on as Madame made her first entrance (a
good managerial idea, to let her see the imposing crowd
in front) the frocking made the “preserved” seats look
very gay and festive.
Contrary to other experiences, the rags are the last
things one gasps about at Bernhardt’s performances, and
I was only vaguely conscious in a hypnotic sort of way
that in the first act of “Camille,” the lady whom our
most distinguished politician has taught us to call La
Bella Frongsay, wore a pale • yellow silk gown
covered with cream silk muslin, with train of silk fancy
tartan in pale harmonious tints, held at the waist by a
jewelled clasp; blouse-fronted bodice, with trails of
yellow flowers from the left shoulder to the right hip, and
white feather-fan slung on to the right shoulder with
bows and ends of broad white satin ribbon (it’s well to be
precise in describing a little easily-copied trick like this).
She also wore her own henna-dyed hair, arranged in a
very sober manner, giving the effect of a “cropped ” top-
knot beginning to grow. In Act 2 the bodice and train
were of shrimp-pink, covered with white net designed in
crystal bead flowers, with two long ends from the front of
the bodice of pink velvet, held at the top with garnet
clasps, the entire front of carubia ottoman silk tied across
with carubia ribbons some inches below’ the waist, short
sleeves of fine white lace, and neck flounce of pink chiffon
—red shoes and hose; her cloak of scarlet, lined with
cream and bordered with red ostrich-tips, was very
handsome. In Act 3 the lady is supposed to have
renounced Worth of Paris, so the gowning was a
simple and artistic garment of cream silk, nicely-
draped on the skirt and contrasted with beetle-
brown silk and cream chiffon, brown hose and shoon.
In Act 4, the—well—disrobing of one of the company
agitated all the opera-glasses within range, and really, I
couldn't help holding my breath at the possibilities of the
landscape if one of those sleeves had slipped. It certainly-
showed the local low-tide corsage the way, but, as excuse,
the culprit had a pair of white and dimpled shoulders. La
Bernhardt herself bounded across the horizon in pink silk
covered with chiffon silver spangled and trimmed with
silver lace, train of black silk, falling unattached from the
waist lined with pink and edged with pink feathers, the
dress tied in round the hips with three bands of black
ribbon ending frontally in a bow and ends caught with a
silver clasp, pink feather fan hanging by black and pink
ribbons from the shoulder, and a mazy array of
“ diamonds ’’—though I really don’t know whether they
merit turned commas or not. In Act 5, the realistic
death-scene (could anything be more natural than the
peevish punchings of the pillow, the restlessness, the
listless endeavour to put her arm into the sleeve of her
mantle ?) was played out in a white muslin robe-de-nuit
with over-robe of white, edged with white swansdovvn.
During the evening numerous floral designs and
bouquets were handed up to the leading lady, and
altogether the Bernhardt premibre was a big gun.
Just as rekerky and crowded a house faced the first
Bernhardt staging of “La Tosca” on 9th inst. as on the
palpitating first night—with the difference that the
applause was louder and more hysterical than on her first
appearance—proving that the Queen of Tragedy has a
good grasp of the Sydney public. Her dresses didn’t help
her much on this occasion. Compared with Copyist
Cora Brown-Potter’s magnificence, Sara suffers from
collar to hem, but, then, her drapings are part of herself
and don’t keep you figuring how much a yard they
cost, as Potter’s did. In the first act of “Tosca,” Floria
wears a pale-green gown with a floral design in green and
pink slightly looped to reveal a jupe of green and
pink, belt of green ribbon ending in long green and pink
bows’ and ends; little spangled “Tosca” bag (they are
already wearing them in the circle) and monstrously
ugly hat in green beaver trimmed in a Guy Fawkes manner
with feathers in pink and green; the sap-green and
brown shawl passed only once over the arm and still not
slipping off. In the Court scene the fffte frock was of
greeny-drab satin, spotted all over with gold tinsel
flowers and bordered with a deep fern pattern in
gold; the skirt, half showing of white tulle,
spotted in gold, with a border of gold—half of the
bodice being of tulle and half of satin: across the bosom
from shoulder to waist a trail of yellow roses and
leaves, the draperies caught on the right hip with an
emerald-green spray studded with diamonds like
dew-drops ; the hair worn simply in a bunch of hanging
ends at the neck. By the way, in the Court scene (where
handsome and fascinating Mdlle. Mea wears a rich gown
as the Queen) it rather spoilt the final grouping for all the
women to have blonde wigs. In the remaining acts La
Tosca wore the chippiest get-up of them all. The slip
of white satin veiled in greenish Tosca net with a foot
bordering of green maidenhair fern and fringe, the
belt and frontal bow of green ribbon, cloak in heliotrope
silk lined with pinky heliotrope floral silk and edged with
deep bands of ermine.
In the audience the dressing was sprightly, but I
saw one or two bogie women. A ham-fat matron in
black and pink towering over a meek little man was
highly reminiscent of “ Catherine ” Fortescue ; another
obese and stage-obscuring party, in brown and a wild
design in lace chrysanthemums, had a battered butterfly
hanging out of her head at the end of a long hairpin, and
a smifky infant, in pink, was transmogrified into a panto,
mime fay by a sort of crown of glittering diamonds ! By
the way, Duquesue didn’t hook the wavering affections of
the feminine portion of the house as “Scarpia.” “He
isn’t as handsome as Curly Bellew,” said a fair somebody
behind me, and of course that finally settles bis histrionic
hash with the “ V ’’-shaped “coterie.”
As befits an all-wool Russian tigress, Bernhardt’s ward-
robe in “Fedora ” certainly ran away from all the pretty
frocks that went before. The first thrill (doesn’t it seem
rather cruel to diagram these glories, to the probable dis-
content of a feminine community erstwhile feeling quite
brave in veiling and “Libbity?”) had the long .“Bern*
hardt ’ train (i.e., only attached to the waist) in dull
yellow satin with a handsome border of large white
“ snowballs ” and leaves and edged with yellow feathers—
the satin spotted at intervals with yellow flowers with
gold centres, the train lined with brown velvet. The
front of yellow gauze, thickly embroidered with the yellow
flowers which edged the low bodice, the bauds of yellow
ribbon about the hips caught frontally with a diamond
dagger. The second toilet proved even more becoming.
Entering, Sara removed a splendid cloak in blue velvet,
with cape of blue moire edged with gold fringe with
collar of black fur, and lining of blue silk, to reveal a
gown of black jetted tulle with train of black silk, lined
with turquoise blue and edged with black feathers;
the invariable hoop of ribbon round the hips held
with a pearl dagger and some black pom-poms ;
blue feather fan attached by bruise-coloured ribbons.
In the breathless “et Louis rcste chez Fedora ” scene, a
spring-green silk is chosen, covered, sleeves and all, with
ecru guipure, the gown caught round with a ceinture in
gold and opals, the train and one side of the front lined
with green silk, the neck finished with a frill of chiffon.
As it seems de rigueur to expire in white, the last act is
fought out iu white brocaded satin edged with brown fur
over a front of primrose silk veiled in creamy lace, lace
sleeves and Egyptian girdle in dull silver. On (he first
night of “Fedora” (three premieres in succession being
rather heavy on the local daily critics, who were kept up
evolving gushing adjectives until 3 a.in.) the usual dressy
crowd marvelled at Sara’s genius from the reserved seats.
Lady Scott (with the Rear-Admiral) had a bad place at the
side, and wore black with a cream crepe front; Lady
Innes wore black lightened by scarlet poppies; and two
well-known medicos’ wives passed the whole of one inter-
val each with her finger in her mouth.
Not being deeply in a diagramatic mood, I am about to
generalise in a dreamy, poetic tone over the yards
and yards of Oriental stuff which Bernhardt hoops round
herself as Cleopatre—for you can’t call them dresses, and
report says they are fixed on with nothing but pins.
Imagine a nude figure wrapped round iu some effective,
unearthly maimer in cobwebby stuffs of a different hue
and design in each act, but always worked with glittering
gems and gold and silver—in one instance, the webby
fabric drawn Over the flesh-coloured stuff, as if it were
the only intervening layer between Cieo. and the balmy
breezes —a Frenchy effect, causing a great clicking of
lorgnettes, you bet. The rosy pedal digits - painted like
the nails—also roused a little mild excitement in a i
enlightened community, which usually begins taking off
its tilings at the top. Different ornaments are worn with
these glittering swathes. In one scene, her long, hanging,
Indian-red hair is adorned with a silver snake, and waist
and fingers are zoned with glittering “jewels” that savour
of the property-room. In another, trails of very real-
looking pink roses, with a golden zone to accentuate the
pretty fall in the back. All through, Cleo. is draped to
soothing point by Sara, and to attempt to analyse the
tout ensemble, couldn t be done without breaking into
poetry, something l ; ke this :
With the sinuous line of limbs revealed by the veil of gold,
Each lissom turn traced out ’ueath the fabric skilfully
wound.
Draping and beautiful form, each of the self-same mould,
Body and silvery web together as one inbound.
Neat, isn’t it? But I will spare you the other ninety-
seven verses.
Harping on the French dramas, what do the idiots mean
who buy weak Gruudyfled editions of the play and read
them with close attention even when the red-haired
Melpomene (I copyright this term) is filling the stage with
her genius? Why don’t they get them off by heart at
home? or wrestle with them between the acts? What
point is there in knowing just what she’s saying at the
expense of losing the cream of the whole thing—the
facial expression and the acting ? On the first night the
circle at times looked like the reading-room of a
library.
With due natural diffidence I must object to one or two
little points in Madame B.’s make-up as Camille. The
youthful pink of cheek and chin ended so sharply as to
form a distinct line against the white of the neck, and
the effect was mask-like. Also, the painting of the rosy
fingers (nails and tips looking as if dipped in ink) was so
crude as to he quite visible without the aid of opera*
glasses. This may be high Parisian art, but it isn’t
human-nature—Sara’s presiding idol in everything else,
seemingly.
From a feminine point of view, the “at home” and
banquet which signalised the official opening of the
splendidly solid “Australia” Hotel on 11th inst. was a
novel orgy at which a mob of women sat about in the
ladies’drawing-room and listened to the rattle of knives
and forks in the great dining-hall until the speeches came
on. Then a move was made to see how the lions were
feeding, and we stood grouped behind Parkes and Dibbs
and Toby Barton while obliging waiters flitted round with
fizz, and I (at least) drank to the success of the “Aus-
tralia” in the safe shadow of a broad, black, satin-covered
back. I had a capital view of the wise en scene—
especially of the top of old Sir Henry’s head, where the
Southdownish mane is thinning out sadly, revealing
fleeting glimpses of a pink cranium. The tables
were nicely decorated, but lire solid floral mound in
front of Mr, Gorman was irresistibly like a baby’s
grave, and the speeches were dreadfully hackneyed. Light
refreshment was spread for the lady visitors in the “late
breakfast room,” and intermittent music kept them
awake in the drawing-room, where the guests were
received by Mrs. Garran (in black lace), Mrs. Ben. James
(purple velvet and mauve silk), and Mrs. Gorman (laven-
der grey silk trimmed with jewelled passementerie;
diamond ornaments). Major Dane’s future wife was there
in a white gown girlishly garlanded with natural flowers,
and the handsomest dress was a rich ivory-satin brocade
with berthe and sleeves of white chiffon and sparkling
diamond necklace. But what spoilt the feminine aspect
of tilings was that most of the women didn’t know one
another, and as no Australian female ever chums in with
her neighbour (except over the back-fence) without a
ceremonious introduction, a damp and depressing st illness
gave the festivities quite an “ after the funeral ” air.
Sir Charles and Lady Hall 6 farewelled to a splendid
hour*-’ mi Uth inst- amidst a generally-expressed regret
that no extension of the season was promised. Both the
Hallos were smothered in Bowers—the “ knight ” receiv*
ing a crown of violets, though goodness only knows what
use he was expected to put it to. It would have been a
graceful thing had he returned with it on and bowed his
acknowledgments, wouldn’t it ? What really pleased me
with some of the audience on the organ seats was that
when certain bouquets fell on the stage, one or two gentle*
men advanced and picked them up for Lady Halle. It
looked quite like continental courtesy (courtesy is mostly
impudence, by the way, while “ rudeness ” is often only
another name for modesty)—altogether different from the
afraid-to-say-his-soul’s-his-own Australian manner on such
occasions.
The tragical gun-explosion on hoard the Cordelia has
damped the eye of many a Sydney damsel, since the late
officers were great favourites—Lieutenant Hillyar
especially having won his way everywhere on his first
visit last year. Whoever remembers his clever fooling in
the Cordelia burlesque, “ Ali Baba," will remember hi B
husky'gagging to (1 think) Cassim ; “ You didn’t know it
was loaded, in coorse.” The words have a fatal tone
about them now.

Lodge Gladstone Masonic Ball, at West's Booms, on 7th
inst., was a well-attended festivity, evidently greatly
enjoyed by the participants therein. The pale-blue and
white moire, trimmed with silver, regalia of the Masons
was very pretty, and most of the ladies were brightly and
festively attired. The supper table was draped in white
and scarlet.

“Great Parkes” the big Sydney Post-
office clock-bell, is now in its place on the tower.
Great Dan, the N.S.W. Postmaster-General, is
now pretty nearly out of his place.
“ Bob ” Smith has resigned his temporary
office as N.S.W. Crown Solicitor, and the staff
heaves a great sigh of relief, for R.M.S. was
a martinet and a glutton for work.
One of the greatest artists of the Sydney
land-boom —the blue-eyed speculator who did
such clever things—has had to call his creditors
together. On paper, he is only deficient £4OOO.
The S.A. labour-leaders endorse the views
of Mr. Charleston, a local M.L.C., who is against
the immediate federation of Australia, on the
ground that the Federal Parliament would be run
in Conservative interests, as only wealthy men
could afford to attend. Then why not make pro-
vision for proper payment of members in the
Constitution Bill?
Harry Stockdale’s Northern Territory
buffalo-shooting expedition leaves Sydney on 28th
per Menmuir, which has had to delay her
departure. Among those who are going are
W. H. Hicks, jun., Stephen Brown (son of the
late Stephen Campbell Brown), W. T. Wright,
of Marrickville, Waiterus Brown, and Marsden
(Branxton). The expedition takes along a photo-
grapher, and means to come back with a steamer-
load of trophies of the chase.
Five country carriers were recently
brought before the beak at Napier, Maoriland,
charged with the awful crime of driving their
waggons on the Sawbuth. They were on their
way from Napier to the inland Pafea district with
goods, and continued their journey on the Sunday.
They were each fined one shilling with costs.
About the same Sunday the Honourable Dick
Seddon, Minister for Mines, and suite, were Hy-
ing across the country by special train. The
Honourable Dick was not fined.
Philanthropist Ardill has got a verdict for
£3OO and costs against the Australian Workman
(Sydney), which bad denounced him as a fraud
and impostor and various other things, and his
adopted niece, Miss Gertrude Williams, has
accepted a plaster of £5O and £BO costs to heal the
wound caused by certain nasty and unfounded
remarks made by editor Higgs concerning her
alleged relations with her adopted uncle. The
Workman simply hadn’t a shadow of a legal case
and rested its defence on the irresponsible cackle
of one or two old women. The whole thing is a
caution against amateur journalism.
