Outside Sydney Her Majesty’s on Bern-
hardt’s opening night seethed a street-jamming
crowd, thatafterwardsburstthrough the vestibule’s
scarce-open gates, trampling down the leaders
who stumbled, to storm the ticket-offices with the
urgency of conquerors just entering a long-
beleaguered city. By seven o’clock the theatre
was crowded except in reserved portions (all the
stalls were reserved except three back rows), and
the close-packed gallery (not vocally disposed to
■emulate Melbourne in the matter of the Marseil-
.laise) kept itself in spirits by singing “ Annie
Rooney ” and the “ Bogie Man,” only leaving off
to cheer Carrie Swain and other favourites, poli-
tical and theatrical, as they entered the
•circle. The curtain having risen to the
traditional three thumps of the Porte St. Martin,
the ovation that greeted Sara on her entry was a
•thing for recollection. She responded in the mode
■so much written about, ere rapidly doffing the
Bernhardt to don the Camille. This reminds us
that the S.M. Herald's late critic has, so far,
■avoided comparisons with Mrs. Potter; although
tie previously stated that the B-P’s slavish replica
was superior to the Bernhardtian original.
Superior, yes, just as the pulings of a love-sick
girl are to the passionate ravings of a goddess.
■Sara, the sinuous, in “ Camille,” as in “La Tosca”
■and “Fedora,” disarms criticism. She magnetises
•you from the first, and even the long waits (much
shorter, by-the-way, than those of Melbourne),
.and three o* 1 four curtain-raisings after each
.act, fail to break the spell. She is not
•an actress but an enchantress; you cannot stop
to enquire why she does things after a certain
•fashion, or if that fashion be the right one.
The mere fact that she adopts a certain mode makes
•that the right one. There is no hesitation about
her, no pause; as an American critic puts it:
“Celerity of action, both physical and mental,
supplies the fascination which attends Bernhardt.”
She is not supported by any great actors or
-actresses, but they are all artists, even though
'some of them look as if they’d come to Australia
to wear out their old boots. Up to date people
have been most strongly repelled by the Armand
•and Cavaradossi of Fleury. An actor whose
rgait is as sloppy as his garb; he might try
,putting his knees in splints as a cure for
-ambling. Angelo, Bara’s juvenile lead ten years
-ago in London, is a lumpish de Varville,
;and the quarrel scene with Duval was not
.-so well done as in the Bellew season.
-Piron’s fine enunciation and artistie ways, espe-
.,-ially as Duval pere, have made him a great
• favourite, just what the unctuousness of Muuie’s
• humour and breadth of style have done for him.
!Du jnesne’s Bcarpia is a fine performance, but not
-so faithfully conceived as that of Bellew. A chief
-ofipolice should be secretive, restrained, cynical,
■ full of reserved force and quiet intensity. His
Tpanoff was fine, the pathos of his cry, “Ma
■mire,” conclusive. Mine. Grandet s Prudence is
younger, quieter, and not so hungry as of
■former exponents. The Queen in “La Tbsoa ”of
Mdlle. Mea was harmonious and stately, while
tthe Comtesse Olga in “Fedora ” of Mme. Gilbert
<was clever in another way ; but the blonde of the
•beautiful bosom, who sat at the head of the card-
rtable in “ Camille,” took the opera-glasses
■off Sara even. The French don’t seem to
i pay the same attention to scenic detail that
■we do in Australia. A pair of palms, a
bearskin and some cane chairs, followed Sara in
'“Camille” from canary-cage to salon, thence to
ilove in a cottage, and finally to death in a garret.
iS'J'or are they, apparently, partial to slow music.
:By the way. Bellew’s condensation of the two
-.scenes in “ Tosca’s ” fifth act was a distinct im-
provement.
•# ■# •
The cash returns for the first four nights
:iare stated to be greater than during a eorrespond-
■ing period in the Marvellous City. So far
■audiences have followed Sara through her won-
drous enunciations of an unknown tongue with
u-apt attention, nor have they failed to listen
quietly to the other members of the company.
The only exception to this rule has been provided
'by folks who attend apparently to study French,
and who prefer to conn their guide-books to hav-
ing the dialogue interpreted for them by watching
the facial expression of the world’s greatest actress.
When several hundred persons turn over leaves
simultaneously, it wafts us back to the days of
school-tasks and prayer-meetings.
* * *
“ Cleopatra,” put up on Monday for six
nights and a matinee, is locally voted quiet, and
•considered to be more of a spectacle than a play.
The Cleopatra of Bernhardt is very graceful and
very charming, but lacking in that majesty which
is essential to the pourtrayal of the queenly and
classic. The scenery, particularly that of the
terrace at Memphis, is fine, but shows consider-
able signs of wear and tear. Darmont’s Antony
has not created a sensation, and the ladies con-
sider him a trifle long in the neck for a profes-
sional beauty. It is not known yet when
■“ Pauline Blanchard ” will be staged. By the
way why do they make Sara’s performances,
already not followed with ease by most folks, less
understandable, by omitting all mention of where
the scenes are laid from their programmes?
** * *
Flying from Justice,” by the author of
‘“Turned-Up” and the adaptor of “The Pick-
pocket,” is a chaotic and incomprehensible
crudity, interspersed by hackneyed situations that
were intended to be sensational, hung on lines that
were meant to be humorous or striking, but are
merely silly and hysterical and pervaded by
characters who resemble nothing in the heavens
above on the earth beneath, or in the waters under
the earth. The murders in this drama are
numerous, and it seems probable that the author
would have slain all his creations in the first act,
had he not started out to construct a four-act
abbatoir ; he therefore prudently saved up a
few victims for the last act, which ends hurriedly
with the death of everybody and the arrest of the
remainder. “Flying from Justice” is_ not a
fashionable piece, and so the dress-circle at
Sydney Royal has not been over-filled since it was
.put up, and the few who do drop in there usually
drop out early, “flying to drink” to alleviate
their miseries. The stalls, however, have been,
so far, well patronised, and the gallery has
been crowded out by the melodrama lovers of
two houses. The leading parts are handled by
the Brothers Rignold, Harry Jewett, Jack
Sweeney, Jim Hazlitt, Joe Tolano, Stirling
Whyte, Frank Hareourt and Arthur Greenaway,
with the Misses Bland, Tracy and Watts-Phillips.
Nevertheless, nothing very complimentary can be
said about any of them. For this all-round lack
of excellence, weakness of opportunity is doubtless
responsible. The scenery is by Geo. Gordon, and
the play is likely to run for some time.
* * *
At Sydney Garrick “Our Flat ” still sur
vives, for the very sufficient reason that there is
nothing in hand to replace it. The management
are still hopeful that it may yet hit the public
taste. Nothing new can be said of the show, ex-
cept that Polly Emery (Mrs. Diver) has been com-
pelled to resign the slavey’s billet for maternal
reasons and that Maggie Knight fills the vacancy
satisfactorily. The little lady is said to be doing
well.

Rumours have been circulated aneut the
secession of a popular American comedian, from
“Evangeline,” still doing tremendous business
at Sydney Criterion. Rumour, however, is usually,
if not always, a liar, and this latest theatrical on
dit is doubtless as little to be believed as any
other peripatetic falsehood extant. On Saturday
evening “ Lone Fisherman ” Harris, diving from
a rock after whales, strained the sinews of his
ankle, and Charles Perry, under-study, had to
take his place, Harris has since returned to duty.

Air and fire have entered intp a cpinpacfc
to ruin water. The week before last a cyclone
tore Coogee Aquarium up by the roots; on
Saturday evening a conflagration levelled Bondi
Aquarium Hall to the ground. The latter build-
ing, the property of the Assets’ Realisation Co.,
was insured for £5OOO. The Chinese epicure who,
according to Charles jLanib, burned houses down
for the purpose of revelling in crackling, has beep
outdone by this expensive mode of providing
boiled and fried fish. Joking apart, however,
everyone will be sorry for Alfred Wyburd, whip
has been dogged by bad luck and who has
worked like a slave, night and day, to make Bondi
a payable speculation. Until the main building
be re-erected, entertainments will be given on the
lawn from a temporary structure,

Bland Holt’s well-balanced company, aftejr
a week’s spell in order to allow the appearance of
the “ Divine Sara,” have again taken possession
of the Adelaide Royal, and “rung” up “The
Golden Ladder ” with satisfactory results. Bland’s
excellent scenery is in marked contrast to the
worn-out rubbish used during the Bernhardt
season, and the acting has been fully appreciated
by the Adelaide public. Bland Holt is ably sup-
ported by Kate Bishop, Walter Howe, Albert
Norman, "Glover, Baker, and others. For the last
week of the season “ Never Despair ” will be pro-
duced for the first time in the Australias. Tommy
Hudson, with a very creditable show, is doing
excellent business a* Garner’s Rooms. J. L,
Lohr is at present in Adelaide on a hurried visit
to his wife.
At Poverty Point.
The Ireland benefit, writes “Dramaticua” to Thb
Bulletin, suggests to me—why, 1 know not, or, perhaps,
Ido know—the stage loves of the past, meaning the real
ones, between actors and actresses, known to everyone
behind the scenes; and I will say, without any dis-
respect to the present era, that in the daysof Rosa Dunn,
Julia Mathews, Rose Edouin, Kate Ward and Fanny
Oathcart, the stage of Australia possessed many pure and
true women. One romance floats before my memory
where the brilliant actress preferred the steady, middle-
aged manager to the ardent leading man, and she was
happy. Another where the extremely well-known,
respected, and wealthy manager would have given any-
thing for the hand of the leading lady, but she preferred
the leading juvenile, and they were unhappy. Apin,
where the leading lady disappointed all the professional
danglers by marrying into Collins-street, and they were
as happy as doves in a cage. Again—but never mind the
seamy side, suffice to know some are finishing well.
The senior partner of the legal firm of Elliston and
Featherstone, Hobart, is a grandson of Elliston, the great
English comedian, who flourished about the beginning of
this century. The family all strongly possess the theatri-
cal instinct.
Walter Montgomery was, on his first arrival in Mel-
bourne, elected an honorary member of the Melbourne
club, for the usual term in sucli cases, one month, but he
was not re-elected. The members generally complained
of his “ noise.” He would stay up all night gambling
with Captain Standish and his “What ho 1 Waiter, bring
me another flagon,” was enough to unstring the nerves of
all the old gentlemen of the club, while the sheep lords
and wool kings, who did not know Shakespeare from the
Bible, were entirely disgusted at what they called his
“ ranting,”
J. C. Williamson bids fair to become Australia's
Augustus Harris. J.C., who is now untramelled with
partners, is at the present time running Sara Bernhardt,
the Gaiety Company, the Royal Opera crowd, “ Our Flat”
Company, and a Juvenile Opera Company (in Tasmania).
Henry Meyer, a notorious and wealthy Viennese snob,
lately offered Wolter, the famous actress, 10,000' florins to
kiss the end of her dress. She consented, took the money,
and then took him to a room where dozens of dresses
were hanging on the walls and said, “There, kiss anyone
of those you like I”
The music-hall men seem to die off a lot faster than
matriculated actors, who, for their part, are anything but
three • score - years - and - tenners in a general way. George
Fredericks, “ a Loudon comique ” and afterwards the pro-
prietor of a little hall of his own (until he betted it to
blazes) has gone to join Leybourne, Fred. Coyne, Pat
Feeney, and a host of similar fast-goers, on the Golden
Shore. Rickards, who has “ played for safety ” and not
hung around the London bars all his life, is one of the
very few “ stars ” of 20 years’ standing. It would be in-
teresting to hear ’Arry lecture upon the lives and deaths
of comic-songsters he has met.
Editor Bulletin,— As the widow of Marcus Clarke, a
brother journalist of yours id an old Bulletin
.contributor, I seek your co-operation in letting
the public know the facts in regard to the
several dramatic versions of mr late husband’s
novel, “ His Natural Life,” now being played throughout
Australasia. Although 1 hold, in conjunction with
Messrs. R. Bentley and Sons, publishers, London, a legal
right in the novel in question, and, therefore, in any
dramatised version of it, as recently settled in hnglana,
I reap no benefit from the stage-productions given of it,
except in one solitary instance, namejy, from Mr. Alfred
Dampier. And it is in justice to his honourable action in
the matter, and in defence of my rights, that I am sending
this statement abroad throughout the Press of Australasia.
1 may add that I would take steps to prohibit the appear-
ance of the many unauthorised and illegalversions being
played here and there, but am advised that the companies
producing them are so. ephemeral, financially speaking,
that it would only mean waste of money. And therefore
it is, I now make this appeal to, you, and I respectfully
ask all journals to help me by publishing the facts as here
given. -—Yours, Marian Clarke. Melbourne, July A, 189,1.
Sara Bernhardt refused to leave Adelaide by steamer at
the last moment. All the trunks were on board, and the
mastiff arranged for, when Sara arrived in her brougham.
An inspection of the cabin settled it; and, stamping her
foot, she intimated her intention to return to shore.
Abbey’s protestations were in vain, and all the trunks
Avere disembarked. They say Sara took umbrage at the
treatment of her mastiff on the trip from Melbourne to
Adelaide. Suggestion for minstrel farce : “ Ruled by her
Pup.”
Somebody conceived a very happy idea when Bernhardt
was playing “Adrienne Lecouvreur” in Adelaide. This
play contains the fable of “The Two Pigeons.” Bern-
hardt chose this selection for recitation when, as a child,
she appeared as a candidate at the Conservatoire, v\ hen
she reached this part of the play and had finished reciting
the lines, one of the “gods” liberated two pigeons
carrying a basket of flowers, and the birds flew quite
gracefully to the stage. Bernhardt was for a time
non-plussed, and seemed delighted at this graceful
compliment.
Madame Simonsen has seen our old friend Signor Dond*
in Italy. He flourishes.
Jolly, .drollest of French comedians, died theotherday.
He created the part of Pomponnet in “ Madame Angot,
and sp it happened that Composer Lecocq’s conception of
the barber, a sentimental noodle with a sweet tenor voice,
was changed into a bounding buffoon. Jolly being unable
to sing for sour apples, the beautiful air written for Pom-
ponnet, in the second act, was cut out of the score both
in Paris and London. The English versions reproduced
the barber on Jplly lines, and still denied him his melody,
although at the same time—the time when everybody had
“ Madame Angot ” on the brain—a travelling French
company were making it the great feature of the opera in
Madrid.’ Pomponnet’s romanza, or whatever the musical
jargonists call it, was recently heard at Melbourne Opera
House, during Nellie Stewart’s reign, to the astonishment
of old stagers who thought they had all the “ Angot
music by heart. Alfred Plumpton, having got hold of
the original Lecooq score, persuaded Nellie to sacrifice
low comedy on the altar of harmony and give the part of
Pomponnet to young Jules Simonsen. With a light tenor
voice just suited to his task, he sang the long ostracised
air charmingly.
An innocent voung Melbourne newspaper man
“ Pattie Browne is really great as‘Polly Ecoles. I remem-
ber seeing Marie Litton in this part a good many years
ago, but I think Pattie would compare favourably with
London’s erstwhile pet comedienne.” The chances are
that the Bohemian boy had “got ’em again” when he
saw Marie Litton play Polly Eccles. That late-lamented
lady was of the slim angelic order, and would never have
been known as an actress but for the fact that her hus-
band (Wybrow Robertson) was theatrically inclined, and
“ ran ” her into notoriety. She did eventually blossom
into a manageress, producing old English comedies, with
Kyrle Bellew for leading man, and so forth. But, remem-
bering her face, figure, and stage career, The Bulletin
can’t imagine that Litton played Polly EColes, and will
solemnly swear that she never was “ London’s pet come-
dienne.” No, the callow critic was thinking of Mane
“Wilton,” the original Polly Eccles, and his favourable
comparison of Pattie Browne with the inimitable Mrs.
Bancroft may be excused on the ground of ignorance,
arising from the fact that he never saw that now elderly
lady. Mrs. Bancroft has been the victim of'comparisons
far’more ludicrous than this. Pattie’s style is well within
measurable distance of the greater artiste’s. Melbourne
Bulletin, a monstrosity which has long been dead and
forgotten, once likened Alice Deorwyn to the real “pet
comedienne ”.of days gone by.
It was funny to watch Sir Binjy Binjy and William
Clarke, Bart, “doing” the Bernhardt season at Melbourne
Princess’. Gracious Heavens! how they did suffet, to be
sure.
Sims Reeves and Edward Lloyd were chorister-boys,
ditto our own Armes Beaumont. But then they were not
made to screech soprano solos after their young voices
had commenced to break, a cruel practice which accounts
for the ordinary choir nightingale becoming a vocal cross
between a raven and a handsaw when he reaches maturity.
A London paper describes Christine Nilsson’s figure as
resembling an equilateral triangle, an idiotic simile in-
tended to explain that Christine is very much embon-
point now-a-days. Yet she was slim and spirituelle
enough when she first set up as a rival to Patti, that won-
derful little woman who has been singing away at the top
of the tree whilst dozens of other nightingales have had
their respective days and disappeared. By the way,
Patti’s splendid state of preservation is a nasty jar for the
medical moralists who swear that there can be no health
in a married woman if she avoids having a family. 11 is
to be presumed that the Queen of Song, like many other
gold-coining artists, has foregone the joys of maternity
for financial considerations.

Ada Ward has had no luck since her return to London-

There has been no soubrette in Australia since she left,

Dramatic art in England is suffering severely from the
crowd of amateurs (more or less) who, having money, are
able to elbow real talent out of the way. Something of
the same sort has been seen here. Anyone can be a “star”
who can afford to pay for his own printing.

The Sydney newspaper-owner who wagered £lOO that
his rag could ruin a certain play now running to big
houses, has paid over the money. Newspaper critiques
don’t influence five per cent, of audiences. It’s the first
night audience’s reports that make or mar a show.
At a Melbourne performance of “A Doll’s House,” an
alcoholised bushman present gave vent to a denunciatory
whoop and informed the stage-artists that their conduct
was immoral, and that they were poisoning society with
false notions about marriage. Forty-shillings or seven
days.

When Cooper and Bailey’s show’ came to Australia, their
biggest-billed deception was “ Behemoth ! On earth there
is not his like. See Book of Job. He was brought across
the Sahara Desert in a glass tank by camels.” This
turned out to be a miserable little agouti, or South
American river-hog, about four feet long, in a tank. They
pretended the giraffe was alive, but it was stuffed, and
mechanism wagged its head ; Titania, the magnificent
big female elephant, died in Auckland, through eating
the keeper’s monley-jacket, with phosphorus matches in
the pocket. She was towed to sea, and fishermen reported
on her as a miraculous whale.

POLITICAL POINTS
Payment of members is Democracy’s key
to Parliament.
A lot of Victorian M.P’s. object to the
term Commonwealth. “ Republic ” is much
better.
The leading newspaper of the Anglican
Church, in Melbourne, this month says British
Monarchy is not a necessity.
Sir Julius Vogel, writing to a Maoriland
paper, objects to either an Australian or an Aus-
tralasian Federation as conducive to Separation.
Memo, for the Australian Labour-party :
“A political party without a daily paper of its
own is like the Salvation Army without its big
drum.”
“ Atticus,” in the Leader, says that the
Anti-Federation party in Victoria “is made up
of Sir Bryan O’Loghlen, and, even then, is divided
in its opinions.”
“ Who and what are the People V’ asks the
S.M. Herald. Why, mutton, and bricks and
mortar, and vacant allotments, and rooms at the
clubs. They are the People.
They are inducing President Carnot to
visit England. The exact right thing, but his
reception would be almost enough to blow the
Tory Government out of office.
Parkes, who rarely sticks to a beaten man,
holds on to Dan O’Connor simply because he
considers it impolitic to risk a seat by making a
fresh selection at this critical juncture.
The Cobden Club is on its last legs.
“Their creed has not gained, but has steadily lost
ground even in England, the first of nations to
embrace it,” says London Standard.
“ Rather than see Australia a Republic, I
would pray to God Almighty to send me to the
nethermost hell.” —Mr. Array tage, M.P. for
Grant, Vie. Well, then, to hell with Army tage !
‘ ‘ Are we to see our wives, Mr. Speaker,
instead of cooking our dinners and mending our
clothes, spending all their time in reading the
Blue Books of this country?”— Fish M.P. (N.Z.)
William Ogilvie’s essay on the “ Rights of
Property in Land,” first printed in 1782, and said,
with Dove’s essay on the subject, to be Henry
George's source of inspiration, will soon ’ be re-
issued by a firm of London publishers.
When men no longer possess the confidence
of their constituents they no longer represent their
constituents. That is sufficient answer to those
who sneer at the terms on which the N.S.W.
labour-members hold their Parliamentary posi-
(ions.
Hugh Mosman, the new Q. Legislative
Councillor, makes only the third North" Queens-
lander nominated to the Bananaland Upper
Chamber. Three out of 40; and, in the Lower
House, 17 out of 72. No wonder the North howls
for Separation.
The big defeat sustained oy Parnell's
nominee at Carlow does not necessarily show that
the Irish priests have of late gained any real
access of general political power. It only goes to
prove that they still strongly influence their people
in regard to questions of sexual morality.
S. J. Way, tlxe B.A. Chief Justice, told
the Londoners that Australians werb quite as
loyal as Englishmen, and pointed to the Soudan
as a “noble example.” Way carefully omitted to
refer to the execration heaped by present-day
Australians on all who were concerned in the
business.
In “Eight Hours’ Day,” Sydney Webb
and Harold Cox state that machinery, “the
demon of man’s own creation is the final justifica-
tion of State interference. “If,” say they,
“ machinery lowers human beings to the level of
monkeys, we can at any rate limit the period of
monkeydom.”
Parkes, The Bulletin surmises, reckons
on keeping in office on either or both of two
questions—Payment of Members and One-Man-
One-Vote. His game is to bogusly and pro-
tractedly quarr.el with the Upper House and,
throwing over all unpopular colleagues; travel
along with his tongue in his cheek as People’s
Champion.
The associated Pastoralists’ Unions boast
that they are unconquerable. They are allied to
the Employers’ Unions and to the Steamship
Owners’ Association. They vaunt that any
squatters who remain outside their organisation
will never be able to get a bale of their wool to
market. They claim that theirs is the Samson of
commercial unions.
It is now made plain that no English
capitalist will be so mad as to establish ironworks
in N.S.W. without Protection against the world.
The N.S.W. morning press, which tried to prove
that the local iron - industry would boom with
Freetrade, is now diligently engaged in proving
that it would be much better to have no ironworks
in N.S.W. after all.
National Advocate (Bathurst) advocates
the formation of a Country Party in N.S.W.
Parliament. How many parties in all? Let’s
reckon : Parkesians, Dibbsites, Labourmen,
Borderers, Backblockers, and Countrymen. To
say nothing of Freetraders, Protectionists, Fair-
traders, Prohibitionists, Local Optionists.
The S.M. Herald declares that the 48
Parkesians in N.S.W. Assembly were returned
by 86,260 voters; the 56 Dibbsites by 66,360
voters; the 30 Labourmen by 58,000 voters ; and
the Independents by 13,600 voters. Not at all.
The S.M.H. uses the word “voters” instead of
“ votes,” and that makes all the difference. The
freat majority of the plural voters pluralised for
’arkes & Co.
John Hancock has made a promising start
in the Victorian Assembly by announcing that
he “hates the sight of a soldier.” The House
ought to have responded with a unanimous “hear,
hear,” but it didn’t, which was evidence sufficient
that the Cabbage Garden is in dire need of fresh
elections and a strong Labour-party. A Labour-
party with no unhealthy desire to put their legs
under Hopetoun’s mahogany, you understand.
Sydney D.T. talks of the “ brilliant
triumph” of Parkesian “statesmanship” as
shown in the “virtually unanimous endorsement”
by N.S.W. of his principles of Federal Union.
Who ever denied the abstract advisableness of Aus-
tralian Union ? An equally brilliant “ triumph ”
on this score could have been scored by a deaf
and dumb blackfellow. The fact is that Parkes
went to the electors on his (i.e., Griffith’s) Con-
vention Bill and got thoroughly licked.
The Electoral League of Orange, N.S.W.*
in an address, reminded the newly-elected “Jupp”
Gardiner that he was about to take his place in
“halls made famous by the genius of a Went-
worth, a Dailey, a Martin, and a Deniehy; by
the unselfishness and ability of a Robertson, a
Parkes and a Dibbs.” Why blemish the con-
junction with Parkes only ? What in the name
of Saint Michael and George has John Davies
done that he shouldn’t be wedged in ?

It is cabled that the Morning Post—a
flunkey organ owned by V.R.’s ennobled toady,
Sir Algernon Borthwick Bart.—commends the
action of Melbourne in forming “a National
Association to preserve the national character of
the Legislature.” If the aforesaid Association
(still in embryo, by the way) is to realise Borth-
wick’s notion of the term “ National ” it will have
to be a male Primrose League with Hopetoun as
Perpetual Grand Knight of the Order, or some-
thing like that.
Sydney Echo, which says what its mother,
the Sydney Morning Herald, tells it to say, but
dares not say herself, seeks to arouse “ Christian ”
prejudices by challenging religiously heterodox
and Republican labour-members in regard to the
oath of allegiance. The labour-members have
read history and have before them the example of
Bradlaugh, which is a sufficiently good precedent
for them. The oath of allegiance is at presen t forced
upon all members of Australian Parliaments, and
it being a matter not of free choice but of com-
pulsion, it will be swallowed whole with a snigger
for the necessary end that people of the Echo
kidney may be prevented from monopolising
Parliamentary representation.
Dear Bulletin,— A word in reply to Mr. Mit-
chell as to his letter on the Socialists’ position. It is
admitted that the appropriation by the State of all annual
value of land as proposed by George would completely
nationalise the land. It is also admitted that Capital is
wealth which is used in the production of wealth, and
which takes the form of machinery, tools, and other instru-
ments of production. It will be also admitted that land
is the ultimate source of all wealth. Land must there-
fore be the source of all capital which is a form of wealth.
Therefore, nationalise land and you nationalise Capital. If,
by a stroke of the pen, we could nationalise land to-
morrow, it does not follow' that all machinery, tools, in-
struments of labour— i.e., existing capital—would be
nationalised—it would have to be replaced from that
source of all w'ealth, the land then in the control of the
State, or nationalised, as we call it. Does the State
Socialist want more than this?—Yours, A, G. H.
Queensland Labour Commission laid two
eggs—a majority-report by six female and six
male members, and a minority report by six male
members only ; the former ideally theoretical and
the latter mostly practical. Nevertheless, the
latter contains this oddity :
That compulsory closure of all shops at a stated hour by
Act of Parliament is as contrary to the spirit of British
freedom as was the curfew law, against which and similar
oppressive enactments Englishmen successfully struggled
in the past.
The comparison made is odious. Curfew was an
enactment against the people’s comfort—opposed
to the worker’s leisure. Early-closing is for the
benefit and leisure of the toiler. Melbourne has
tried it after a variegated fashion, and doesn’t
seem to be over-slave-bound by it, either.
The Orange N.S.W. Electoral League
holds that —“Labour is God’s greatest and noblest
gift to man.” Question. Isn’t toothache as noble
a gift as backache ? Labour is such a grand gift
that everybody who can do so refuses to use it.
Even the Orange E.L. wants to limit this
“greatest and noblest gift” to eight hours per
day—on the principle, we suppose, that one can
have too much of a good thing. Can’t the O.E.L.
refrain from talking unsanctified rot ? Even th
Bible, which is often enough queer in its phil-
osophy, sets down Labour as a curse. Down to
now-a-days the man who labours gets all the
kicks, and the loafer who doesn’t, gets all the
ha’pence. We can fairly look this “gift horse”
in the mouth.
Doctors Bergmann and Halim, Berlin,
coolly confessed, last week, that they had experi-
mented with cancer-lymph on human incurables.
Here you have the cynical admission than an
hospital is only a place where the poor are experi-
mented on for the benefit of tire rich.
Mr. H. Valentine Haynes has been
delivered of a pamphlet which he styles—“ A
Machiavellian Solution of the Australian Labor
Problem.” Haynes’ cure is mainly to fire out the
army, which is good enough as far as it goes, but
is bluntly Herculean rather than Machiavellian.
Mr. and Mrs. Jewell were survivors from
one of the Auckland Islands wrecks, and Jewell
became a stationmaster on the Gippsland railway.
They carefully keep as mementoes the dresses of
skins they wore many months on the islands.
Mrs. Jewell was the only Eve amid the poor
starving Adams.

FEDERATION OR. NO FEDERATION.
The latest scheme for Federation
Claims to he for the country’s weal,
While all may prove but botheration,
Which can’t be said of Pe.mell’s Meal.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
’Tis good and pure and cheap I ween.
The Flying Gang: A Railway Song
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Oh, I served my time in the days gone by,
In the railways’ clash and clang,
And I worked my way till the end, when I
Was the boss of the flying-gang.
’Twas a chosen band that was kept at hand
In the case of an urgent need,
Was it South or North, we were started forth,
And away at our utmost speed.
If a word reached town that a bridge was down,
The imperious summons.rang—
“ Come out with the pilot-engine sharp,
And away with the flying-gang !”
Then a piercing scream and a rush of steam
As the engine went ahead;
With a measured beat by the slum and street
Of the sleeping town we fled.
By the uplands bright and the homesteads white
With the rush of the Western wind—•
The fastest engine in all the yard,
And a single truck behind—
And the country children clapped their hands
As the engine’s echoes rang ;
But their elders said, “ There is work ahead
When they send for the flying-gang.”
Then aoi’oss the miles of the saltbush plain,
In the moonlight wet with dew,
Where the grasses waved like the ripening grain
The pilot-engine flew;
And the order sped on the wire ahead—
“ The Pilot must go through.
The Governor’s special must stand aside,
And the fast express go hang;
Let your orders be that the line is free
For the train with the flying-gang.”

Great Truths.
Great truths are very simple truths when
finally they are comprehended; but it takes a
long while to prepare any mind to comprehend
a great truth. For years one may grope in
darkness concerning some phase of his spiritual
life, or some perplexities of a sacred friendship,
or some seeming contradictions in his inner-
most personal character ; when suddenly a
light will break in upon the mind, or upon the
heart, which instantly makes clear that which
before seemed hopelessly dark. A great truth
is then perceived in its bearings upon, and so
in its explanations of, all that was bewildering
and disheartening to one who was in ignorance
of that truth. What a new sense of life and
hope comes with such a new recognition of a
comforting or of an enlightening great truth.
In the joy of an experience like tliis, one can
wait and trust in other perplexities which may
yet be similarly resolved and dissipated. Thus
it is that on the introduction of Clements Tonic
everybody wars sceptical of its merits ; yet now
so popular has it become, and so reliable has it
proved itself, that hundreds of bogus medicines
are placed on the market with which to gull
the public. Every genuine article is always
imitated, and people will always try to impose
on the credulity of their suffering fellow men.
Patients must be on their guard against these
swindlers, for their remedies are perfectly use-
less, and while time is being wasted, the disease
increases, and perhaps even beyond the power
of so potent a remedy as Clements Tonic. Mrs.
Brand, of 33 Australia-street, Newtown,
Sydney, writes : “ I think it my duty to write
in grateful acknowledgment of the great benefit
my mother has derived by the use of Clements
Tonic. My mother was severely ill from in-
ability to eat and general debility ; and in fact
her condition caused us great anxiety ; we had
a great repugnance to using patent mediciea,
but the numerous cures we heard had been
effected by Clements Tonic, and which was
corroborated by our personal friends, induced
my mother to try one bottle. The effect of this
one bottle was marvellous, her appetite has
returned, and her health wonderfully improved.
We never thought it possible that so great an
improvement could have been effected in so
short a time. You may make what use of this
you like, as we are sure there must be many
sufferers, who like my mother, would derive
immense benefit by the use of Clements Tonic.
I am yours gratefully, Evelyn Brand.” Care
must be taken that the genuine “ Clements ”
Tonic is obtained, as the use of imitations must
lead to disappointment and regret.

The “bloody” Chief-Justice Jeffreys once
said to a pert lady-witness; “Madame, you are
too quick.” To which.she retorted: “Not half so
quick as your wife.” Then a great silence fell on
the court, for folks were afraid to laugh in the
presence of the terrible Jeffreys, whose wife had
added to the population two months after mar-
riage.

ho was wont to do so long and vigorously that

Mr. F. Call, as P.M. at Melbourne, once
had before him an alleged quack doctor, charged
with illegally practising without a permit, and
fined him £lOO. The quack instantly flung a £lOO-
- on the table, and another £5, remarking that
the latter was for the “ poor-box.” Call got indig-
nant, and thundered out, “Do you mean to
express contempt for the court? ” “No,” meekly
responded the quack, “No, your worship, I may
feel it, but I can’t afford to express it.”

Another anecdote of Gerard Krefft. Dr.
Benj afield went lecturing round with a snake-
antidote, and was expatiating to a select assem-
blage, at Sydney School of Arts—or somewhere—
when the cry rose from the boys at the back,
“ Here’s Gerard Krefft! ” So Gerard, with the
inevitable gunny-bag under his arm, was passed
from hand to hand, till he bounded on to the
platform. “ I say, Doctor, do you say you’ve got
an antidote for snaag-bides? ” “ Yes, certainly.”
“Den try this one ! ” And out it came waggling
from the bag. The Doctor vaulted to a top shelf.
A southern paper relates that at a Vic-
torian bush tea-meeting at which Bishop and Mrs.
Moorhouse were present the small building was
crammed to the doors and the low platform on
which the speaker sat was also invaded. A young
fellow, attired in striped shirt and moleskins, but
adorned with a flaming red “comforter,” the ends
of which were stowed beneath the waistband of
his trousers, slouched up to a form on which a
plainly-dressed, comfortable-looking woman sat,
and giving her a nudge with his elbow, said,
“Would you mind shoving up a bit, missus?”
“ No, no,” cried the local clergyman ; “ you must
not sit there ; that is the bishop’s lady.” “Non-
sense !” said Bishop Moorhouse, who sat next his
wife, “ squeeze up a bit, Mary.”
Writes a Bulletin London correspon-
dent :
Edgar Ray, once of Sydney Punch and afterwards of
London Tomahawk, is irrepressible. He is now the
secretary and manager of a London company which
undertakes the winding and synchronising of clocks. He
is quite a reputable member of society, and his daughter
has made a good match. Did you ever hear the story of
his being asked to dine at Henry Moore’s, whom he did
not know, and turning up at old Mr. Want’s house, at
Potts Point, where he was also a stranger? Want, who was
the most hospitable man on earth, thought that perhaps he
was a friend of one of his sons who had forgotten to keep his
appointment to entertain him, and so he was made welcome.
Edgar was a cool card, and though he knew he was in the
wrong house, did not believe in hunting farther, so he
waited. When dinner was over, he calmly turned to the
host and said : “ I believe I have made a mistake, and
have come to the wrong house.” Mr. Want replied, “I
think so, too ; but never mind if you have enjoyed your-
self.” And so the matter ended. Edgar loved “ good
society,” and never really had his heart in the Tomahawk.

The cable states this week, with its wonted
vigour, that “the report that Prince Ferdinand
of Hohenzollern, heir-presumptive to the Crown
of Roumania, had been betrothed to the Arch-
duchess Maria Dorothy Amelia, daughter of the
Archduke Joseph Charles Louis, cousin of the
Emperor of Austria, is officially denied.” The
Bulletin officially remarks just here that this
sort of thing is tommyrot, and it doesn’t matter
one turnip whether it is “ denied ” or not. Rou-
mania isn’t much of a kingdom, anyhow—it’s only
a spot; and Ferdinand’s show for the crown
doesn’t amount to much, and it isn’t of the slight-
est interest whether he gets a crown or a corn;
and nobody in Australia knows Maria Dorothy
Amelia from Moses ; and Joseph Charles Louis is
only cousin to a family of lunatics, and it is to be
hoped that he enjoys the billet. And if Ferdinand
had married Maria to-be-oontinued-in-our-next it
is just possible he would have been sorry he did it,
for quite possibly the lady kicks in her sleep owing
to her name lying heavily on her chest; and then he
might have thrown dishes at her ; and, if so,
they are better unmarried; and, in any case,
nobody at this end of the cable cares whether they
are married or not, or whether they are both
smothered, or whether there ever were any such
persons at all, or whether Joseph Charles Louis
is cousin to an emperor or an elephant. It makes
us feel tired, and that is about all.
A Christian Brigand.
REMINISCENCE OF THE KELLY TIME: VERY
SUITABLE AT A POLITICAL CIRSIS.
The Kellys were out and so was I. It was funds
I was out of, and I was padding the hoof from
Melbourne. I shall not take the trouble to prove
that I was much younger in 79 than I am in ’9l:
I was, and that’s enough. I picked up a brother
swaggy at one of Big Clarke’s stations ; he was a
shocking bad cadger, except for tobacco, and of
that he did not get a smoke until I said, “ Let me
ask the next cove and see what luck I’ll have.”
Just then a swell came along, and I touched my
hat and addressed him thus: “Excuse me, sir,
but this here mate of mine has tried every soul
for a bit of ’bacca for the last eight miles. They,
none of them, would give him even an old dog or
the dottle of a pipe. For goodness sake give him
a pipeful to stop his jaw, I’m sick of his whining,
and I don’t smoke. It’s a beastly habit.” Now,
strange to say, the gentleman burst out laughing
and said that I was a brick, but that he had no
tobacco. “However, here’s a shilling. Just on
ahead is Chloe’s Swamp. Buy your mate a fig and
have a drink for yourself.” Off he rode. Then
my mate abused me like a scab-shearer for insult-
ing him, but I tramped on towards the beei’.
Entering a pub. I found that I was still within
the range of threepenny drinks, so I had
a pint, left my swag in the bar, gave
the insulted man sixpence, and proceeded to
explore. J ust then a poster caught my eye :
“Vote for Fiddler, the People’s Friend,” and
again another, “Vote for Parson and Liberty.”
Theseare assumed names, as I fear Sir William
Breezier and libel. Then I turned to my friend
and said, “You wait here and watch the swags,
I’m off to get cheap beer.”
In those good old days there were open pubs for
the clients of the candidates. So I drank to
Fiddler, the coffee and spice-grinder, and to
Parson, who is now a nice, respectable Minister of
the State of Pigtoria ; then I repeated the doses
and went back to see my friend. I was not
drunk, only beery. My mate had explained to a
policeman that we were hard up and were making
up-country, and I find that I also had explained
to another policeman in mufti that I was off
to Goulburn. You cannot imagine how kind my
trap was. He was the sergeant, and offered us a
shake down in the lock-up, which we took. He
shouted half-a-dozen times and asked us a hundred
questions, always eyeing my mate’s concertina-
case and a parcel I had in my hand. It was hlack-
pudding. They gave us a candle and the news-
papers. They even told us that Fiddler or Parson
was at the head of the poll. But they drew the
bolt on us at night, and I could hear my friend, the
Sergeant, sending off and receiving telegrams.
Still, we slept the sleep of the tired and beery.
In the morning we were asked how we slept, and
the police gave us three shillings to get our break-
fast and laughed at the concertina and sausages.
They said they were disappointed in us, because
they hoped to sell us for a couple of thousand
apiece. Complimentary !
I forget how I headed this yarn. Oh,
yes ! I’m a bit of a brigand, and I was once a
Christian. There was a crisis in those days : a
fair and square beer and whisky political crisis.
What the gambling den is a crisis? Anyway,
crisis beer is as good as any other sheoak. It’s
scarce in this here crisis—worse luck.

Umslopoghaas, the Zulu companion of the
late well-known journalist, D. C. F. Moodie, is
now a miner on Broken Hill. He has married a
white woman and is a happy and proud father of
four piebalds. By the way, Moodie did not walk
from Adelaide to Melbourne (as we notice most of
the papers saying), but went there by steamer,
for the occasion of caning the late A. T. Clark.
Melbourne Clubmen got up a purse for him, as a
token of their “loyalty,” and the overland walk
was the journey back to Adelaide.
Editor Bulletin.— Sir,- I can scarcely hope that you
will publish this, because it is an adverse comment upon
one of your would-be witty items. In your present issue,
11th July, you have a very Spiteful paragraph about
“Bagmen.” Now, I had an idea that you had a “down ”
upon our craft, on account of an ill-judged resolution of
the C.T.A. anent your “ Jubilee ” number. But I did not
think that your bilious attack was so bad ; and I trust to
that “spirit of fair play,” of which you boast, to get at
least a show. Only one very ignorant of N.S.W. com-
mercial travellers could have written the paragraph in
question. Anyone conversant with the “roads” knows
that, as regards the “ pie ” business, we usually come in a
very bad second to squatters and their satellites, com-
mission-agents and bank-clerks. As to the Bruce Smith
incident, only a new-chum traveller would take the
trouble to write to you about such an ordinary occurrence,
as one of us, who pay, having to stand aside for a dead-
head M.L.A. Impartial inquiry would prove that we are
the Railway Commissioners’ best customers, and most
entitled to consideration. As to the “snag” business, I
venture.to remark that it is lucky for the Hon. B. S. that
he did not encounter one of our “ old warriors,” or he,
might have “struck the snag.” As one of the oldest
travellers, I write “as one having authority.”-Yours,
Semper Paratus. Sydney, 10th July, 1891. [We are
afraid the drummers understand none but their own
jokes.— Ed. B.j

A pamphlet has been Issued by the Department
of Agriculture containing exhaustive articles on
■pig-rearing, pork-making, &0., by Mr. J. L.
Thompson, Principal of the Hawkesbury Agricul-
tural College, and Professor E. M. Shelton,
Instructor in Agriculture for Queensland, together
with an interesting American official report on the
position of the pig trade in that'country. These
articles not only give information as to the various
breeds trf pigs, but describe in detail the best lands
of food and the mode of constructing the qaarters
which arc most conducive to the successful and
rapid development of the best qualities of this
valuable animal.

Society
How vain even clever men may be of
petty titles was illustrated a few days since in the
case of Rudd v. The Commissioners of N.S.W.
Railways. The recently-knighted “Sir” Julian
Salomons, Q.C., was counsel for the Commis-
sioners. and having occasion to refer to himself,
observed : “ My learned friend in reply may say,
‘ The argument of Sir Julian was to the effect
that’” , &c, “Sir Julian’s” learned friend
did reply, but always alluded to the little knight
as “my friend.” Which reminds us of a story of
another N.S.W. knight who stopped the ’bus at
Rushcutter’s Bay, a few days after his title was
announced, to tell the green grocer in a loud voice
from the box that Lady wanted to see him.
*' * *
A just-arrived Englishman, an Oxford
graduate, by the way, who had been walking
round Sydney, dropped in at The Bulletin
office one day last week. “Tell me,” he said,
“who was this Albert the Good to whom you
Sydney people have erected statues, and so on?
Was he in the meat-preserving? Pardon mj
ignorance, but we hear very little of your great
Australians in our country.”
* # •
That night in the last short session of the
N.S.W. Parliament, when the Speaker’s casting-
vote saved the Government from defeat, cards
were being- vainly sent in wholesale to members
by those who desired to view the proceedings. At
last a messenger approached Minister O’Connor,
cardin hand, but was rebuffed with a “Don’t
bother me, sure the House is full, I can t look at
it.” “But this,” persisted the attendant, “is
from the Marquis of Huntley.” “What!”
shouted the P.M.G., “the Marquis of Huntley!
Why didn’t you tell me that before ? Where is
he?” Then the Jaynial Dan elbowed his way
through the corridor to the crowded corner where
the mysterious Marquis kept a group agog.
“ Delighted to have the distinguished honour
of ” —1 said he, then broke off shortly to remark
in an aggrieved tone, “ Tom Huntley, you divil,
you’re always at your thricks.” “The Marquis
of Huntley” is better known as Tom Huntley,
prominent Sydney butcher.
*' * *
The committee of the English Bar have,
it is cabled, resolved to discourage the practice
of sons who are barristers appearing in
courts in which their fathers are presiding
judges. And while the English Bar Com-
mittee are about it, they will, on the same grounds,
have also to discourage the practice of sons-in-law
appearing before their fathers-in-law, also judges’
nephews appearing before their uncles. In Aus-
tralia, we have contrasted the son effect with the
son-in-law effect and find that the latter “takes
the cake.”
■t # #
It is hard to see, however, what can be
done in the matter. It by no means follows that
the son of a clever judge is bound to be a bad
lawyer, Then, as to judges’ legally-successful sons-
in-law, there is no law to prevent a barrister from
falling in love with a judge’s “elderly ugly
daughter,” and it is to be presumed that a wise
judge, so far as he can control such affairs, favours
the love-suit of the cleverest aspirant.
* # #
The conduct of attorneys prima facie cer-
tainly lends colour to tiie nasty insinuations you
so often hear concerning the partiality displayed
by the Bench for its professional relations. Where
is the attorney who, when his case comes before
his Honor, loses any opportunity of “pleasing”
that functionary by giving his son or son-in-law,
or nephew, a brief? And where, for the matter
of that, is the layman who doesn’t think he gains
a point by employing a solicitor who is the judge’s
relative, or particular pal, to appear in the suit
over which his Honor presides? Yet, if you
asked us to name any judge now on the Aus-
tralian Bench who is notoriously influenced in his
judicial duties by his professional relatives we
couldn’t honestly do so.
* # #
One thing we should like to know : What
is the etiquette observed in the privacy of the
domestic circle between judicial father and barris-
terial son or son-in-law, or between judicial uncle
and barristerial nephew, concerning cases in
which the former has to preside and the latter to
appear as advocate? Do they never mention such
things, or do they, after the ordinary fashion of
human nature, give each other the “straight
griffin?” Whatever they do or do not do, it cer-
tainly is most uncomfortable for the litigant who
has not been so fortunate as to secure the Judge’s
relation to plead before the old man, to see his
enemies and the Court troop along to dinner as a
happy family party.
# # #
Apropos , we once saw a man who was a
fellow-traveller by coach with a Judge on circuit
carve the mutton at dinner, and, seeing that his
Honor liked his meat well done, gushingly volun-
teer to pass along to him “a little more of the
brown,” which the Judge gratefully accepted.
Next day, when Regina v. Brown, sheep-stealing,
was called on, Mr. Brown (the carver), who had
been on bail, stepped behind the spikes and
greeted his Honor with what was rather a nod
than a bow. He was acquitted, and the Judge,
with a twinkle in his eye, told him that he had
had a very narrow escape, and that it should be a
warning to him against placing himself in any
position in which he might fairly be suspected of
having carved another man’s mutton.
# # #
A haughty Victorian School-Inspector,
while inspecting a country-school kept by a lady,
was offered a cup of tea. He raised his eyebrows,
but, as the day was hot, he accepted the beverage
in dignified silence. Ten minutes after his
departure, a small package of “ post-and-rail ” tea
arrived from the nearest store —Jib. of “ ten-
penny,” value 2|d.—with this curt letter : “Memo
for Miss , I drank your tea because you are
a lady, but a man in my position could not
possibly accept so small a favour from one of my
subordinates. ”
# # *
A feature of the baccarat case was the
neglect of Lady Coleridge to stand up, like every-
body else, when her husband entered court. Her
disrespect was very womanly and natural, after
I all. She forgot that Coleridge was Lord Chief-
Justice, and couldn’t see any sense in rising from
her seat because the “ old man ” had arrived.
Which reminds us that it must be a severe trial
for certain vice-regal ladies to get upon their im-
perious feet when a henpecked Excellency comes
along to open Parliament, or perform any other
function at which his wife is an ordinary spectator.
But they have to do it. Mutual reverence amongst
royalties and vice-royalties is the secret of their
greatness, and the real value of suites lies in the
persistency with which they raise their titles and
kow-tow to the powers that be-
HOBSON’S CHOICE.
HE (under a glaring lamp in the Park) : “Here is a vacant seat, Miss Boodle-shall we
take it ?”
SHE (pctulantly) : “Well, since all the others seem to be occupied, I suppose there is no
alternative.”
London newspaper reports of the baccarat
scandal case, and their comments thereon, quite
bear out Bulletin theories. The young men who
swore they saw Gumming cheat were accused by
plaintiff’s counsel of having been mildly intoxi-
cated all through the Doncaster race-week, and
the influence of champagne upon the female
imagination was remotely hinted at. Also, it is
suggested in more than one journal (even the
Argus “social gossiper” goes thus far), that a
Tory Solicitor-General was not the man to cham-
pion Gumming against the Prince of Wales and
party. He merely put the Heir Apparent in the
witness-box for the purpose of whitewashing him
as far as was possible under (or over) such dirty
circumstances.
9 * *
Colombo is fast becoming notorious for
suicides. “Dr.” Drummond, of Sydney, who
levanted with his wife’s trust-funds some time
ago, took poison there ; then Phil. Beck blew out
his brains on board a P. and O. boat as she was
entering Colombo harbour ; and now Davidson
and the erring Miss Ayliffe, of Adelaide, have
added their names to Colombo felo-de-ses. It was
at Colombo that sculler Searle caught the fever
which ultimately proved fatal.
9 9 9
Sara Bernhardt appears to have con-
ceived a great dislike to the Adelaide society
people. On the day of her departure all the smart
crowd hied to the local railway-station to grasp
the hand of the “ divine ” and bid her an affec-
tionate and sisterly good-bye. But Sara went
to the station about a quarter to three, and, enter-
ing her carriage, drew the blinds and resolutely
refused to see anybody. The crowd was disap-
pointed, and the train left the station amid a
deathly silence,
# # •
Dr. Hinder is going to lecture the Austral
Salon (Melb.) on the “ History of a Slain Infant,”
of course referring to Daffy’s Elixir, Mrs. Choke-
kid’s Soothing Syrup, and so on. But why not
take up Mrs. Grundy’s cold-blooded murders of
the poor servant-girls’ babies?
# * *
The Bulletin notices that a certain
Sydney building society, which is in difficulties
and cannot meet the demands of its depositors,
still brazenly advertises for deposits and offers
alluring rates of interest. Perhaps when the
directors of this institution find themselves on
the public roads, they will recognise the fact that
there is such a thing as a criminal law. There is in
N.S. Wales at present absolutely no law to pro-
tect the provident worker from the operations of
such sharks. The present audits are mostly
shams,
* * *
“When Greek meets Greek.” On 3rd
June, at the Leichhardt Hotel, Rockhampton,
Dr. Spiridion Candiottis went to his last rest. In
every part of Australia there will be Bulletin
readers to notice this with interest, for many were
the men in many paits who knew “ Old Candy.”
Practising in the fifties on the old Victorian gold-
fields, afterwards on the Lachlan, then residing
in Sydney, he eventually followed the Baroona
and Peak Downs rushes, and settled at the latter
place for the rest of his life. A Greek of the Her-
culean type with the constitution and strength of
an elephant, the companion of Sir Julius Vogel
on the Lachlan and afterwards the friend of gold-
warden Griffin, the tales that were told of him
would fill a book. One of the best known was
related in the Australasian a few years ago by
David Falk, who, doubtless, thinking the hero of
it long since defunct, headed it with the real
name : “ Candiottis, the Greek.” Kind friends did
not fail to supply him with copies of the paper,
but, although the eyes gleamed under the shaggy
eyebrows, the old man was wise in his generation
and said nothing. A fanatic over whist, ho was
such a terror to his partners that at one time
the whole district unanimously refused to play
except against him, and it is said that the only
time he fairly met his match was when he inad-
vertently sat down to play Sir Arthur Palmer.
He had other little weaknesses. He once tried to
sell a friend of the present writer a book of which
he had two copies. He said, “ That book is new,
it cost seventeen shillings, the postal order that
I sent was fourpenoe and the stamp twopence—
that will make seventeen and six.” Said the other,
“But aren’t you going to charge for the note-
paper and envelope ! ” But with all his little
failings he was emphatically a strong man, as
many have found to their cost.
9 9 9
Beaney’s will remembers the diamond-in-
terest in bequeathing £25, for a ring, to Widow
Lachal, almost the only living reminder of the
Doctor’s diamond firm, Sylla Denis, Victor Denis,
and Gustave Lachal. The Beaney retainers and
hangers-on benefit but little under the will, and
listened to its reading with blank visages like
Sir John Vesey, Lord Glossmore, and Sir
Fwedewick Blount, in Lytton’s “Money.”
# * *
Beaney would give reporters any amount
of gratuitous treatment, and we have heard an
impecunious journalist say, “Beaney is always
good for a £lO note, if a man has the cheek to go
and ask him.” Beaney’s gratitude to a section of
the press, and hatred of another, dated from the
Lewis trial.
# # *
A fair creature in Queensland asks The
Bulletin to publish seven sheets_ of foolscap
written all over on both sides setting forth her
grievances against her husband. She says that
after their marriage the old man led a very giddy
and erratic life for years, but, falling seriously ill,
he suddenly manifested a most tender affection
for her. “He got very frightened,” she says,
“ and was never satisfied if I wasn’t at his bedside
holding his hand.” But all this suddenly changed
as soon as he was convalescent. Two days after
he arose from his bed of sickness he appears to
have taken a red-headed woman out for a drive.
“Not,” writes the injured wife, “that I would
have minded that so much, but the cat actually
came in a boastful way to me, and, bold as brass,
told me that she spent a very pleasant afternoon
with Teddy.” Our beauteous correspondent asks
us for advice. We will ponder over the matter
for six months.
# * *
Lady Lyfcton not only gave Sara Bernhardt
a pressing letter of introduction to a prominent
leader of Melbourne society, but wrote herself,
backing it up. The leader in question, however,
managed to be “out of town.” What is the
matter ? Are we too Calvinistio now ?
# * *
The big Melbourne drapery business of
Buckley and Nunn has been sold to a syndicate
for £350,000.. stock,lockandbarrel. Whereby hangs
a tale. For more than thirty years Buckley managed
the show in Melbourne, whilst Nunn bought for
the firm in London. They never nut. Once
when Buckley had booked his passage home,
and was on the point of starting, he suddenly un-
packed his trunks and stopped' where he was, to
the great grief of his employes, who loved him
dearly and thought a nice sea-voyage would bo
good for everybody’s health. Finally, Nunn
astounded the rag trade by turning up in Mel-
bourne, a few months ago. Having wept on the
bosom of his long-lost partner and taken a look
around him, the mysterious Nunn returned home,
his death being announced by cable very shortly
afterwards. As a matter of fact Nunn drowned
himself in the river Avon, a stream .otherwise
famous for a certain immortal “Swan.” Then
there was no Nunn at ail, and the old business
passed into new hands last week. Sic transit
gloria nunndie—no, mundi.
is # *
The majority of women who patronise the
stalls at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, to see Bernhardt,
remove their hats. If some of them would remove
their hair as well, or, still better, leave it at
home, the “ back-seaters ” would have further
cause for thankfulness.
A MELANCHOLY EVENT.
A ballad of an Emperor who travels from afar
Upon a “brilliant” visit to his good old grand-
mam ar ;
He lands in Merrie Hingland ’midst a burst of
“loyal ” whoops,
All his uncles being present with a quantity of
troops.
Before he lands, God bless him ! he is greeted on
the brine
With a naval demonstration by the vessels of the
“ line ” :
The bold marines are mustered in the bows and m
the poops— .
For there’s nothing pleases emperors like ironclads
and troops.
He compliments the country on the men he sees
arrayed
(Who, at twenty pounds per annum, are a trifle
over-paid) ; . ...
He slyly turns to Tummy, and a royal eyelid
droops
As he whispers, “ I say, nunky, do you often lead
the troops ?”
They take him off to Windsor to be welcomed by
the Queen
(There’s a dozen special artists making sketches of
the scene),
She wants to kiss his forehead, so he dutifully
stoops,
And opens up the subject of those everlasting
troops.
Dear Granny, who’s domestic like, as “ loyal ”
papers tell,
Delights to ask her William if his little ones are
well,
She talks of royal infants, and their measles, and
their croups,
“A babe in arms,” says William, “ sets me think-
ing of the troops,”
They form a grand procession, and they march
away to dine,
The uniforms and ladies make a spectacle divine 5
They discuss a gross of courses after starting on
the soups,
Yet the flow of conversation never gets oeyond
the troops.
And when the feed is finished this remarkable
young man
Suggests he’d like to see as many soldiers as he
can ; , , , .
So all the Royal Family they stand around in
groups
Whilst Vicky shows her relative the body-guard
of troops. * *

Bowen is the Geelong of Queensland.
A Chinese druggist advertises “pills that
will change gills into fine healthy boys.”
Says Edison :
It is my intention to have ready for the World’s Fair
such a happy combination of photography and electricity
as will enable a man to sit in his own parlour and behold,
depicted upon a curtain before him, the forms of the
players in an opera upon a distant stage and to hear the
voices of the singers. When the system is perfected,
each little muscle in the singer’s face, every, glance of the
eye, and each expression will bo seen at work. Every
colour in the performer’s attire, too, will be exactly re-
produced.

Re’s up at six next morning; in a military suit
He swaggers round the barracks where the warriors
salute;
Anon he swaps his tunic for the one with bigger
loops,
Then clanks away with Tummy to inspect a lot of

# # #
A petition for a reduction of the mon-
strous sentence of eight years’ penal servitude
passed on Haynes, defaulting land-agent at Hay
(N.S.W), for a small embezzlement (his first
offence), has been largely signed in that district.
The public have failed to comprehend either the
justice or the necessity of this crushing penalty,
especially so since Dulhunty, the defaulting
Corowa land-agent, the other day got only one
year for much more serious deficiencies. It is an
ugly coincidence that Dulhunty, who is “well-
connected,” was a “ society” man who gave cosy
dinners at the public expense, and had many
friends and much back-stairs influence. Such
things would, of course, not weigh with 1 the Judge,
who, it must be confessed, gave defendant what
was, under all the circumstances, a sufficientlv-
severe sentence ; but why so savage a penalty in
the case of Haynes ?•. ~ “■> ■
* * m
In March it was urged that two members
of the N.S.W. Rifle Association should be com-
pelled to retire at the end of one instead of two
years. Lieut. Hammond, says a contem-
porary, suggested that the matter should
remain in abeyance till the end of the
year, as some one might drop out. He, unfor-
tunately, was the first, being blown up at Middle
Head. Then Commander Lewington was asked
to name the other, and he put it off from week to
week until he, himself, made the second one, he
having been cut to pieces on the railway near
Arncliffe a week or two ago.
# * #
A laudable practical joke was recently
played upon a N.S.W. official who has a large num-
ber of minor appointments in his gift, and who has
the credit of giving billets to men—not too inquisi-
tive-possessed of attractive wives. A smart young
man wanted a billet, and hearing of the official’s
“peculiarity,” engaged a very pretty member of
the demi-monde to personate his wife. She waited
upon the official, succeeded in getting the appoint-
ment for her temporary hubby, and ingratiated
herself with the big man in a way that he will

* # *
Pathetic chunk sawn oft’ an address on
the “Side Lights of City Life,” delivered before
the A.N.A. (Melbourne): “My friends, a sobbing
barmaid in one of the principal hotels in this city
told me, while tears choked her utterance, that
unless she drew more trade her services would not
be required.” He didn’t say what became of the
girl after that, but we presume he led her gently
away by the hand, and she became a reformed
character, and taught in the Sunday-school, and
after a while she eloped with the parson, when he
skipped with the trust-money belonging to sundry
orphans. When a man goes round sympathising
with weeping barmaids it always ends in some-
thing like that.
* # #
On a rough estimate, about 75 per cent,
of unmarried male hospital patients finish up by
proposing to the nurse, provided the nurse is a
female and reasonably good-looking. Conse-
quently, it is a nicer job than it looks to be a good
Samaritan in an hospital. When a fellow has
nothing to do but lie in his bunk, and groan, and
take medicine, it generally happens that he must
fall in love with somebody, if it is only to kill
time, and when he is visited at intervals by a
slender female in a graceful costume he naturally
begins to feel a poetic yearning underneath his
mustard-plaster. Being in a weakened and dis-
organised state, he is capable of yearning over any
maiden under sixty, and the average nurse is a
good deal under it. And, moreover, her hair is
nicely arranged, as a rule, and she mostly has a
wistful, sympathetic expression in her eyes, which
makes her look as if she was thinking about
poetry, when she is only smothering her feelings
about the troublesome patient who is sure to
holler for her under the impression that he is
dying just when she wants to sit down and have a
rest.
* * *
According to Sydney Presbyterian, five
available Church of England parsons, including a
Bishop, refused to officiate at a recent Moree
N.S.W. funeral when asked to do so in the
absence of a “dissenting ” clergyman. The excuse
given was that the ground was not consecrated.
An orthodox parson can only pray on holy ground
and according to routine rules. Some parsons
never pray outside of their surplices.
* * #
The wife of a well-known and lately much-
advertised high, N.Z. functionary, is a niece of
Tom Sayers, the prize-fighter. She seems to have
inherited much of the family’s bull-dog tenacity .
for, divorced from her first husband, a blacksmith,
she married a lawyer, and then, when he moved
from this vale of tears, espoused his partner—her
present husband.

Eight young lady doctors, University-
trained, come into practice in Melbourne next
November.

A girl was brought up at Sydney under
the Vagrant Act last week. A solicitor asked
that she should be remanded for three weeks, as a
young man named M‘Ginty, who was present,
was willing to marry her. The Bench assented
and (thus are prophecies fulfilled) “ down goes
M'Ginty,”
* * *
Bringing up the rear of an eight-hours’
procession at Maryborough (Q.) recently was a
dray on which were a large marble cross, a sculp-
tured urn, and other cemetery decorations, ex-
hibited by a local monumental mason. Attached
were the legends : “One people, one destiny”—
‘ The world’s workers need rest.”
* * *
Ballarat’s 68 hotels have been voted down
by the local optionists at one fell swoop to 28, the
statutory number per population. With compen-
sation for the extinguished and a roaring mono-
poly for the 28 left, the publicans ought to be
resigned to their awful fate.

Sydney Evening News advertising baby-
market is just now in an active condition. The
Inspector-General of Police might do worse than
tell off one of his smartest detectives for a year to
watch the operations of that interesting institu-
tion. Also, the Evening News is just now very
full indeed of the “young lady” who wants to
borrow £5 from “a kind gentleman.” The other
evening, by the case was reversed,
thus :
WANTED, kind young Lady to LEND young man
' ' Few Pounds ; repaid quarterly instalments.
Quarterly, E. News Office.
You see the lady must be young ; an old woman’s
money won’t be accepted. Bet you men of this
kidney will do better at the game than even the
distressed “young ladies” have done. We pre-
sume that the detectives keep their eye on this
class of appeal. Inspector Camphin, it will be
remembered, arrested those cruel murderers,
Nicholls and Lester, purely through an idea which
occured to him on reading an innocent-looking
advertisement, inserted in all good-faith by the
S.M. Herald.
# * #
The alleged fact that a pious “ smarty ”
solicitor, in the employ of a Sydney legal firm,
whose piety has brought it, well, quite as much
of its business as its legal acumen, has just
victimised several wealthy Wesleyan clients of
his employers, for some thousands of pounds, and
then levanted, reminds one of the malicious story,
well-known to the members of the Articled Clerks
Association, about the man who once sued for
slander the other who had called him a Wesleyan
—“meaning thereby,” &o. The continuation
may be fitted in according to taste and experience,
but, in this instance now under notice, the
ordinarily-inserted innuendo fits like a glove.
* * *
The well-managed and prosperous Com-
mercial Banking Co., of Sydney, has, it would
appear, had its little turn at last. It accuses
Robert Charles Cole —a youth, now committed for
trial, who joined its service in 1884, at £5O a year,
and who, when he retired recently, enjoyed an
annual salary of £l9l ss.—of forging warrants for
£8857, which amount he appears to have duly got
in cash from time to time. The detectives are
just now busy in trying to find out where the
money has been planted. It is alleged that the
bank’s total loss through the recently-discovered
system of warrant-forging amounts to, at least,
£lB,OOO.
* * *
Not Charles Strong, but his lieutenant
(Alexander Marshall) is the best-advertised Mel-
bourne parson now-a-days. The eloquent plati-
tudes of this “ advanced ” apostle, honest and
practical enough as sermons §o, are published by
Melbourne Heeald as quite the newest thing in
revelations. Monday’s Heeald, glittering with
gems of thought like “Rev. Marshall says that
every man can do something towards making
society better,” or “In the opinion of Rev. Alex.
Marshal], the world requires more brotherly love
than he sees displayed ” is uncommonly suggestive
of a penny tract. There are extenuating circum-
stances to be found in the evenin’ horgan, how-
ever. Side by side with exhortations to search for
the “ higher life,’’one gets full details of a criminal
assault in lower life, so the whole may be likened
to a ham sandwich, or rum and milk.

couple.

The alleged fact that all the German,
Austrian, and Italian soldiers are to be put into
impenetrable cuirasses, reminds one of the Life
Guardsman from Waterloo, who, when the
Prince Regent, who had a cuirass fad, asked him
how he would like to fight the battle o’er again
replied, ‘ ‘ In my shirt-sleeves, sir. ”
The full report of the late naval battles on
the Chilian coast is interesting reading for those
Australians who are very much inflated about the
new and glorious ironclad navy which has just
been laid on. The scrimmage began by the insur-
gent warships O’Higgins and Blanco Encalada
blockading Valparaiso and Balmaceda’s squadron,
consisting of one little gunboat, the Mary Florence,
and a torpedo-boat. These two tiny vessels
rushed out on a forlorn hope one dark night, and
in about two seconds the gunboat was a mass of
undistinguishablo hash, and everybody on board
was knocked into pie; while as for the torpedo
craft there wasn’t enough of it left to scratch a cat
with. These two boats probably cost about as
much as the N.S.W. Freetrade surplus amounts
to in twelve months, and they only gave some-
thing like half a minute’s fun altogether. Next
minute a shell from one of the forts struck the
O’Higgins somewhere about the stomach, and
it looked as if the Last Hay had broken
out on board. It probably took two years
to build that ironclad, and it cost about as
much as the price of 50 miles of railway, and
three-fourths of it went into the air in fireworks
with a single shot. Two or three days later the
Blanco Encalada was lying off Caldera, when
something else happened. She was the pride of
the whole navy—about £500,000 worth of pompous
ironclad in one heap—and two little torpedo-
boats came along and sunk all that money right
off. There was just one ghastly roar, as if a fire
had broken out in Tophet and all the devils were
rushing for the door, a great mass of shattered
iron, a big feed for the sharks, and then the
captain swam ashore with half his clothes blown
off him, and howled with rage on the beach, and
all was finished. This ironclad business doesn’t
promise to pay.
“Big-Head."
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Labour Leaders [—Labour Members !
Fill your story ;
Kindle Hope’s pale glimmering embers
Into glory.
But, in Freedom’s promised morning,
Mark the False the True suborning—
Pits are digged ;
So a word of friendly warning—
’Ware of “ Big-Head" !
Labour Members I—Labour Leaders !—-
Be no shirkers;
Champions of the People—Pleaders
For the Workers !
In your greatness still be simple,
Lest success your manhood rimple—
Tory Whig-head !
Plebeian plainness mustn’t pimple
Into “Big-Head.”
Delegates, whose lives are blent with
Labour’s payment,
In the forum be content with
Labour’s raiment.
Wear no symbols of the shopper,
Harmony with self is proper—
Labour rigged ;
Drop the frock-coat and bell-topper
Of the “ Big-Head.”
Listen ! every Labour Member—
Seize your Birthright;
Plutus lives his last December—
Free the earth-right!
Manhood suffrage your defence is,
Sweep away all Class pretences—
Licensed Pig-head!
Yet, in victory, keep your senses—
’Ware of “ Big-Head" !

294 Castleresgh-street, June 25, 1891.
Radam’s Microbe Killer Co., Gentlemen, —For
forty years I have been a sufferer from rheu-
matism, and latterly in the most aggravated form,
often having acute attacks which made me almost
helpless for days, insomuch that it was with the
greatest pain and difficulty that I could move
about at all, and only able to get up stairs on my
hands and knees. A friend who had tried the
“Microbe Killer” with beneficial results sent me
a jar of the medicine, which I took, and I have
now taken three jars of “Microbe Killer” and
the result has been a great wonder to myself and
my friends, for now I can get about freely, with-
out pain. I go up and down stairs without any
trouble, and I am greatly improved in health
every way. In fact my suffering from rheumatism
seems a thing of the past, and I can recommend
the “ Microbe Killer ” to anyone with confidence,
knowing what it has done for me. Several have
tried your medicine on my recommendation for
other diseases—and one a cancer patient—with
the same beneficial results.—Yours respectfully,
MRS. WHITAKER.

The Bulletin notices that a N.S.W,
coroner the other day dispensed with an inquest
on the bodv of a man who died suddenly, on the
report of aconstable who had made a post-mortem
examination, and “ found no marks of violence on
the body.” Good. We have known constables to
run in as drunk, men with skull-fractures who
showed no marks of violence.
A collection of instruments of torture is
now on exhibition in London. We haven’t seen
them, but we believe that they include Ban
O’Connor’s “ spache,” the suburban rooster, the
howling infant next door, the female with her
hair flying who recites “ Eugene Aram’s Dream,”
the ungreased wheelbarrow that goes along the
street just at day-break, the tram-whistle, the
woman in the next house, the Salvation band,
washing day, Abigail’s orthography, Reid’s eye-
glass, Duncan Gilbes’ smile, Munro’s Scotch
accent, Thomas Playford’s feet, the water-supply
that shuts off just when you have soaped your
face and are getting under the shower, Slavin’s
portrait, the leaders in the S. M. Herald,
K.C.M.G.’s, the Orange Lodge, the A.N.A., the
Primrose League, our tailor’s bill, Parkes’ hair,
the rush,for victuals at a Government House
ball, and the Governor’s centless sympathy when
anybody is killed in a mine.
