The Distribution of Wages—The
Bachelor.
Dear Bulletin, — If it be admitted that barely
sufficient wealth is produced on this oblate
spheroid which wo inhabit to supply the wants of
all its inhabitants, and if it be further admitted
that one great cause—indeed, the secondary cause
—of poverty is the unequal distribution of that
wealth, then it is surely evident that any attempt
to improve the prevailing distributive system, or
to show how it might be advantageously altered,
must prove beneficial to the bulk of the com-
munity. Undoubtedly the principal absorbers of
the wealth which ought to be fairly shared among
all are the monopolists, but even that portion of
wealth which, under the name of wages, escapes
their greedy grip, is unequally apportioned among
those who lay claim to it. Now, it having been
acknowledged that the food supply is limited,
and that unfair distribution is one cause of
poverty, it must be conceded that the system,
which pays the labourer simply according to the
amount and quality of his work, while totally
ignoring his liabilities and responsibilities, is
unjust and productive of much evil.
# # #
On arriving at manhood, the earnings of the
young worker are usually greatly increased, but
should he then, obeying the promptings of Nature,
take unto himself a wife, thus increasing his
expenditure at least 50 per cent., no additional
allowance is made. Should his wife, in obedience
to the teachings of Holy Wr.t and natural law,
from time to time augment the population, thus
increasing his expenditure at least 10 per cent, per
annum on the amount requisite for his support
alone, still no further increase to his income is
made. The man who served his apprenticeship
alongside of him, who attained journeyman’s
wages at the same time, has remained unham-
pered, has given no “hostages to fortune,” and
accordingly has not a tithe of his responsibilities
and outlets for expenditure, yet is paid at the same
rates. Should the bachelor be saving and long-
headed, he can not only put by a pound or two
weekly, but, by judicious investment, may become
ip a few years an employer of labour himself. No
such door of esaape is open to the married pro-
ducer who has a large family,; he is over-
weighted, completely handicapped out of the
race of life. Should the bachelor be fond
of amusement, he can “fly round and see
life,” while the married drudge has not only all the
joy crushed out of his existence, but is positively
in many cases unable to provide his growing little
ones with healthy accommodation, sufficient
nutriment, and the careful training necessary to
the formation of good citizens. Is this fair?
Should not the earnings of the married toiler be
increased in direct proportion to the number of his
family, even if the receipts of the bachelor have
to be curtailed somewhat in consequence ?
* # #
Here the single man, who is selfish, illogical,
and unphilosophical, interjects—“ That would be
unfair; Ido just as much work as paterfamilias;
I demand the same wages.” Of course the stupid
man does not understand the matter; it is not a
matter of remuneration, not a dispute between
man and man ; it is a matter of necessities—one
between mouth and mouths, between back and
backs. Has the single man more bread and butter
than he can eat, more clothing than he can wear,
or their monetary equivalent?—if so, he is
depriving those, mayhap unwittingly, who have
not enough of these necessaries of what
they are justly entitled to, from the mere
fact of their existence. If the grasping
bachelor is enabled through the folly of existing
social conditions to overfill his dehiscent maw
by intercepting that food which would otherwise
reach starving stomachs, it is surely time that
system was renounced in favour of a more humane
and less fatuitous one. Moreover, if we consider
the community aright, it will be seen that it is
nothing more nor less than a huge family, and that
many of the social evils arise from neglect to take
this into account. Viewed from this standpoint,
it will be seen that the children of each family are
the sons or daughters of every grown-up person in
the community, the brothers or sisters of all the
rest. Until this be fully recognised, the ill-fed,
ill-housed, ill-educated, ill-reared youth of each
generation must become a source of never-ending
expense, a pestilence, a curse to the generation
that follows.
• * •
You, the clamorous, responsibility - shirking
bachelor, are the cause of much of the misery we
have to face. For those denied by you, and
such as you, the opportunities to become good
citizens, we build gaols, erect gallows, and whip-
ping posts. For those whom your companion-
ship has ruined, we build hospitals, benevo-
lent asylums, lying-in institutions, and found-
ling homes. In order that your animal pro-
pensities may be indulged, oh, brutish bachelor,
our streets are.nightly thronged with those for
whom no gladsome wedding-day will ever dawn,
for whom, through your greed, the door of no
honourable occupation may ever be opened. Yet,
oh whited sepulchre, you dare to say that pater-
familias should be compelled to support the results
of his marital felicity, while knowing that your
action denies him the wherewithal, meanwhile
complacently deeming that the State is bound to
provide for the victims of your unhallowed inter-
course and economic thefts. It avails you not to
say that all married people are not chaste. Your
action in remaining single, in evading responsi-
bilities, in taking that to which others have a
better claim, is to a great extent the source of all
immorality. Were the community’s offspring
reared properly, there would be fewer victims
ready to fall a prey to your fleshly desires, and
thus nature would compel you also to marry, and
accordingly by means of early marriages the State
would not be unduly taxed for the support of
these institutions which your profligacy makes
necessary.
* * #
Besides, the bachelor does not directly pay a fair
quota towards the expenses of the State and
municipality in which he resides, for he contributes
but little towards the payment of rates, and the
other burdens which fall on the shoulders of the
house-occupier and tax-payer.
# # #
That the share of wealth received by the married
man should be greater than that apportioned to
the bachelor is as necessary in a civilised State as
in a barbarous one. Here we may learn much
from the savages who divide the spoils of the
chase, and the foray among the tribe in direct
proportion of food to mouths, not so much con-
sidering who did the work as who possesses the
appetite. Surely, if it be admitted that the land-
lords, usurers and monopolists, absorb an undue
amount of the common stock of nutriment, it
must also be acknowledged that the bachelor
withdraws from the general fund more than twice
what is absolutely i-equired for his support, while
creating by his profligacy evils for whose allevia-
tion the necessary funds are subscribed by
the entire community. Denying his obliga-
tion to contribute towards the support of
the community’s young, he carefully spreads
the weight of his obligations over the
shoulders of all. “ Bear ye one another’s
burdens,” said the founder of Christianity, but
the bachelor not only absolutely refuses to bear
the burdens of others, but undeniably shirks the
full weight of his own.
# # #
If the appearances of possessors of labour-power
in the labour-market are to be regularly sustained, i
then the present sellers of this force must be
afforded healthy opportunities for perpetuating
themselves. Labour-power continually withdrawn
by tear and wear must be as continuously replaced
in quantities at least equal to that withdrawn:
hence the sura of the means necessary for
the production of labour-force must, if only
for economic reasons, include the means neces-
sary for the support of future labourers. Thus
the married producer performs a dual duty; he
not only labours for the support of the community,
but has a mighty trust in his hands as the rearer
of the future generation. He is working, not only
for the present generation, but also for the next;
yet the shallow and unthinking bachelor fails to
see this, and would fain deny the father’s right to
receive more from the common fund than he does,
not that he personally has not enough, but from
a dog-in-the-manger desire to hold on to as much
as he can. What is required is not so much a tax
on bachelors, not so much a diminution of the
earnings of single men, as an increase of the pay
of the married producers. If their pay cannot be
increased, then let the few suffer in order that the
entire coim* unity may be benefited. Then if the
single man desires to be put on an equal footing
with other men, his remedy is at hand while there
is a single girl in the community.
* # *
Until it is universally recognised that each
member of the community is to a great extent
dependent on every other, and that the ill-health
and discomfort of one must be detrimental to the
good health and happiness of all, progress will
ever be delayed. Surely it is the duty of each
generation, as trustees for the next, to hand over
to them fuller liberties than they inherited, more
happiness,' more opportunities of acquiring scien-
tific and general information. Until this be fully
acknowledged the selfish man will continue to
grab all he can, without considering that someone
may be more in want of it, and thus the criminal
ranks of each generation will continue to be
recruited from that which preceded it, thus will
the present system of distribution continue to
create prostitutes and weaklings, while overbur-
dened parents are hurried out of the world leaving
behind them ill-trained, half-grown orphans to
become a burden on the State. Is it any wonde r
that marriage is not a popular institution ? Is it
surprising that so many refuse to accept its cares,
its responsibilities, recognising that the sexual
pleasures on which it is founded can be enjoyed
at the public expense ? Surely not! Until some-
thing is done to popularise marriage the “Social
Evil” will flourish, criminals‘will abound, and
the coming of the millennium be indefinitely post-
poned. The Neo-Malthusians are, so far, the
only body who have dealt with the question from
the standpoint of present cofiditions, and there-
fore their doctrines are worthy of consideration.
Future reforms may wipe away any necessity foi
interference in this matter, but under existing
social conditions a re-dutribution of the earnings
of labour, giving a greater share to the more
heavily burdened would be beneficial. Mean-
while, force and stupidity govern the world until
right and intelligence are ready to assume the
sway.—Yours,
PATERFAMILIAS.
“THE BULLETIN."
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains>
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by compar.ng
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They ai’e now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of, pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress ; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official —that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted'in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kugf.lmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine ivithout it doing me the slightest
good, but 1 became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased cbming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as even'
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
i Peter Smvtiik, J.P.
